LAREDO, Texas, April 8.â Reliable re
ports of a serious riot yesterday at
Lampasas, a station on the Mexican Cen
tral^Railway, seventy miles distant, . have
reached here. It^ appears that the gpv
ernment of Mexico has suspicion about
a reported revolutionary occurrence at
that place and yesterday morning .' the
military stationed there arrested four
citizens. When they were taken to the
station, en route to Monterey, ' a mob
headed by Francisco Maranjo, son of, the
famous General Maranjo, appeared at the
station. In j the excitement that followed
one of the prisoners escaped. When the
train left the leaders of the mob repaired
to General Maranjo's castle, where, they
are at present : surrounded. As soon as
the government heard of the disturbance
500 soldiers from Monterey were dis
patched to the scene by special train.

Outside of politics no fault is found

Cuba. He is believed to be a very good
executive officer. It is not thought that,
even if the army is withdrawn, any
change will be made with reference to
General Wood, but the proposition to get
the troops out of Cuba is being very
carefully considered. .
It Is felt here that General Wood has,
failed to grasp the signification of events.
He has not; been in touch with the con
stitutional convention at all. He reported
to the President and the War Department"
that the constitutional convenion would
surely accept the Platt amendment, and
the final rejection of the amendment on
Saturday came to the â¢ President . as a
shock and overthrew General Wood as a
prophet.
Wood Not a Good Prophet.
General Wood has - since been appoint
ed brigadier general in the regular army
and it is admitted in many quarters that
while he has been an improvement over
General Brooke in a few respects, he has
fallen far short of General Brooke as a
diplomat. Tho administration, while "ex
pressing great â¢ confidence in General
Wood, is beginning to realize that he has
lost the confidence of a very considerable
portion of the Cuban public and has failed
to realize the trend of public sentiment.
General Leonard "Wood, Governor of
Cuba, is likely to resist such a step.- The
President has been told for two years that
one of the greatest causes of friction be
tween Cubans and Americans has been
the military government, yet the army
has. been adverse to letting go and -the
army has had its way. Â¦-'.--. .Â¦ *..

changed Spanish military rule for Amer

consider the great difference between the
methods of Spain' and the United States.
The President recognized this when . he
pent General Wood, a, .volunteer soldier
and a doctor in. civil life, to succeed Gen
frral John R. Brrtoke., 'who .\v3,s 7 <Â» s regular

Would serve with less of the mili
tary stiffness about his .regime and. would

WASHINGTON, April 8.â Secretary
Long was informed to-day that the tor
pedo boat Perry, built by the Union Iron
Works of San Francisco, failed to meet
contract speed requirements on her recent
official trial. Under the contract the ves
sel was required lo develop a speed of 29
knots an hour, but the best she could do
on her trial run was 28.2 per hour. The
failure to xrake the full speed required.
2a knots, will not result in the rejection
of the ship, as the contract provides a
penalty for deficiency in speed between
29 and 2S knots. The boat will therefore
be accepted, the price being $259,000 in
stead of the full contract price of $263,000.

ada, and damaged the Russell House! one

The flre spread from the theater to the
hotel with great rapidity and forced some
of the 200 guests to make their escape in

fusion the electric flight wires- were cut,
leaving the hotel nt .total darkness. All
the guests escaped, nowever, without in
jury. . "The. Belle of. New York" Opera
Company played in the theater last night
and what scenery and costumes had been
left in the building were lost. The com
pany leaves the, city this morning, and
some of their effects had been sent to the
depot.
Besides the hotel and theater, the block
contained the office of the Free Press, and
another portion of it was occupied with
offices. The loss on these is small.

DREAM OF A SCIENTIST
Ohio Man Suggests That Abundant
Ice Be Created on the San Ber
nardino Peaks.
SPRINGFIELD, Ohio. April 8.â Dr. C.
W. Wooldrldge, a well-known Ohio scien
tist, came out to-day in advocacy of a
unique project. He would create a glacier
at the top of the San Bernardino Mount
ains, in California, that would change the
arid lands of Arizona and Southern Cali
fornia into a fertile garden. He would
have the standing army now used to de
stroy employed to Improve, and suggests
as one of its tasks that it erect an Im
mense chimney on the Pacific Coast, of
an enormous height, so that the ocean air
could be carried to the top â¢ of the
San Bernardino Mountains, where by at
mospheric action It would be reduced to
an extremely low temperature, thus form
ing, according to Wooldridge, an Immense
glacier. ;
The presence of this glacier, he says,
would cause a sufficiency of rainfall,
w/iicn in that country is now lacking be
cause the upper stratum of atmosphere Is
too warm. He also suggested electric dy
namos which would furnish power to till
tne field, plows being used that misrht turn
up an acre of land at every trip. The land
wculd be irrigated by underground water
courses, and all over the farms he would
have, trolley tracks so that the farm
wagons could be run easily.

BRIDGEPORT, Conn.. April 8.â Perry L.
John's, the attorney, who returned Satur
day from Mexico to give himself up to
the authorities, was arraigned- in the City

from the Pendleton estate and the Sta
ples Free Academy fund. He waived ex
amination. Probable cause was found and

the Superior Court in $12,000.
ed issue of corporate stock for property
and the issue for monej^ Justice Dlxon
finds, lies not in the rule for valuation,
tut in the fact that different estimates
may be formed of the value of the prop
erty. WhÂ«n such differences are brought
before a judicial tribunal Justice Dixon.
says the judgment of those upon whom
the determining of the value 19 placed,
must be accorded considerable weight,
but it la not final.
Question of Overvaluation.
"Nor is it necessary." the opinion says,
"that conscious overvaluation or any
one form of fraudulent conduct on the
part of these primary values should bo
shown to justify judicial Interposition.
Their honest judgment, if reached with
out examination into the elements of
value or if used in part upon an estimate
of matters which really are not proper
or. if plainly warped by self-interest, may
lead to violation of the statutory rule .as
surelv as would corrupt motive. The or
iginal issue of corporate stock Is a spe
cial function. In the exercise of which
the Legislature has fixed the standard l<\
be observed, and It 13* the duty of thÂ«
courts, -so far as their Jurisdiction ex
tends, to see tiiat this standard is not
violated either intentionally or uninten
tionally."
Justice Dlxon points out that under
section 40, when corporate stock has once
been issued for property purchased, the>
Legislature has directed the application of
another rule. Under such conditions noth
ing but actual fraud In the transaction,
can Impair the right of the stockholder to
hold his stock as full paid stock, free
from further call.
Applying the rule 83 above laid down.
Justice Dixon discusses the actual value
of the property, the purchase of which
was contemplated by the issue of $45,000.
000 of stock. He finds it admitted that the
value of the Guggenheim plant as physi
cal possessions did not exceed $10,000,000.
and with the cash to have been turned
over, leaving about $23,000,000 to be made
up in the good will of the business and
the leases and contracts, the nature of
which was not disclosed at any time in
the proceedings. While the defendants
claimed the complainants had not borne
the burden of proof cast upon them. Jus
tice Dixon holds that under these cir
cumstances this rule should not be vigor
ously enforced at this stage.
Conclusions of the Justice.
The proofs. Justice Dlxon says, point
strongly to the conclusion that in the ne
Â¦ gotiations between the parties the real
value of the property to be acquired had
not been the basis upon which they have
determined the amount of stock to be is
sued therefor. The fact that the expected,
consummation ' of the deal caused the
market value of the stock to rise, and
which was used as an argument that the
proposed purchase would not be advan
tageous to the stockholders. Justice Dlxon
finds to be without weight. Me says that
if the intrinsic value of the stock was
only 60 per cent of its face, and an out
sider offered 80 per cent in money for ad
ditional stock to be issued, such an offer
would clearly be advantageous to the
I company and its stockholders, but it could
! not be legallv accepted, because the Legis
lature has required that 100 per cent,
whether in cash or property, shall be re
ceived for corporate stock.
The court finds that the mere fact that
two-thirds of the directors and stock
holders voted for the purchase can not
prevent its review,- pointing otit that this
would not justify â¢ an issue of stock for
an illegitimate enterprise.
In accordance with these views the court
orders that the stay be continued enjoin
ing the purchase and that proceedings to
increase-the stock should likewise remain,
in statjl quo pending further argument
-and a -decision by the Court of Chancery
as to the real value of the Guggenheim
property. .-;.*,:.;

construction placed by the court on sec
tions 48 and 49 of the general corporations
act. Vice Chancellor Stevens held that the
court could not go beyond the decision of
the board of directors as to the value of
the property to be purchased by the issu
ing of stock. The Court of Errors, on the
contrary, holds that it must be clearly
shown that the value of such property is
at least reasonably near the price to be
paid, and the action of the directors is
subject to review at the instance of any
stockholder who considers himself ag
grieved. Tlje syllabus of Justice Dlxon
follows: : â¢ â¢
Text of the Decision.
Under section 40 of our corporation act when
an original issue of corporate stock for prop
erty to be purchased Is contemplated it is the
duty of the directors to see that the real value
cf the property is at least equal to the face
value -of the stock. Before an original 'ssue
of corporate stock for property to be purchased
takes place the bona fide Judgment of the di
iectors a3 to the value of the property, while
It Is entitled to considerable weight, is not
conclusive, but may' be . reviewed - at the In
stance of existing stockholders, and If on such
review by a court of equity the -value of the
property appears to be less than the face value
of. the stock the issue should be restrained.
After ' stock ; has Â¦ been Issued as fully paid
stock for property purchased the judgment of
the directors as to the value of the property
becomes conclusive In the absence of actual
fraud in the transaction, and such stock is
not liable to any further call. An increase of
corporate stock, voted for by the board of di
rectors and by the requisite majority of stock-*
holders in order to Issue such stock for prop
erty worth less than the face value of the
stock, should be restrained at the instance of
dissenting stockholders.
In .considering the right of the court to
review the action of the directors, Justice
Dlxon says the language of section 4S of
the corporation act is not questionable
as to the fact that the money paid in must
equal the face value- of the stock issued..
The language of section 49 is even more
explicit, providing thaf the corporation
may Issue stock to the : amount of the
value of the stock. ;
The distinction between the contemplat-
sion. The decision of the Supreme Court
was.that "the decree of the Circuit Court
of Appeals should be reversed, with costs
and the case remanded to the Circuit
Court with Instructions -to set aside It3
decree adjudging that the order of the
commission be enforced and to dismiss
the application made for that purpose,
with costs, the whole to be without preju
dice to the right of the commission to
proceed upon the evidence already intro
duced before it upon such further plead
ings and evidence as it may allow to be
made or introduced, to hear and determine
the matter In controversy accordlns to
In the cases of the Clyde Steamship
Company the decrees of the Circuit Court
of Appeals and of the Circuit Court were
modified by providing that "the dismissal
of the bills shall be without prejudice to
the right of the Interstate Commerce
Commission, if It so elects to make an
original investigation of the questions con
tained in the records pertinent to ytio
complaints presented to that body." As
so modified the decrees were affirmed.

War Casualties Are Figured as High
as Fifty Thousand.
"WASHINGTON. April 8.â The rebellion
against American authority has been a
costly experience for the natives of the
Philippines. Fifty thousand Filipinos
form the lowest estimate of the War De
partment of casualties sustained by thÂ©
Filipino forces; 7667 rifles have been cap
tured or surrendered and 605,142 rounds of
ammunition, as shown by incomplete re
turns, have been seized. The number of
Filipinos killed cannot be accurately de
termined by the department, as General
MacArthur in his dispatches states that
It is Impossible â to be accurate on this
point, but it would not surprise officials
shculd losses sustained by the insurgents
in killed and deaths from disease reach
25,000, and some say that 50,000 is closer to
the real figure. .
The rebellion in the Philippines has been
In progress for three years. In his annual
report for HS99 Major General Otis, then
in command of the archipelago, said that
"Aguinaldo's concentrated army was. var
iously estimated at from 20,000 to : 30,000
men. Officials of -the War Department
now admit that while this estimate may
have been true of the army proper, it is
certainly not true of the strength of -the
Filipino forces throughout the insurrec
tion, as the disaffection resulting from
Aguinaldo's efforts and his impressment
of natives caused large additions to . his
command.
Adjutant General Corbin is satisfied that
tho casualties suffered by the Filipinos
will in themselves form a potent reason
for the abandonment of further resistance
by the natives. The progress made in
pacification Is illustrated by the figures
in - possession of the department. For
twenty-seven months, ending November
30 last, the total number of Filipinos
captured or surrendered was 13.4G8 officers
and men. and the number of rifles turned
in 4933. Since November last 6600 officers
and men have been captured or surrend
ered, and 2734 rifles have been delivered
to or captured bjÂ»the American troops.

COMPLETE VICTORY
FOR INSURANCE MEN
Nevada Supreme Court Declares the
Valued Policy Law to Be
Invalid.
CARSON, April 8.â The Supreme Court
of Nevada to-day rendered an opinion on
the valued Insurance policy law which
was passed over the Governor's veto by
the late Legislature. The court de
clares the law to be Invalid, as It. lacks
the necessary signatures of officers of the
Senate and Assembly. Secretary of State
Howell refused to certify to the bill when
he discovered the absence of the sig
natures. The case was then taken to the
Supreme Court on mandamus proceed
ings.
Thetlecision was rendered by Chief Jus
tice Mossey, Judge Belknap concurring,
and Judpe Fitzgerald dissenting. The
Court held the law to be Invalid, quoting
section 8 of article 4 of the State consti
tution, which requires the signatures of
officers of both Legislative houses when a
bill is passed.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, April 8.â Dawson passengers
of the steamer City of Seattle, which
reached port to-day, say that pneumonia
is prevalent in the Klondike. Colonel J.
C. McCook, United States Consul for the
Klondike, was very ill on April 2 and had
practically no chance of recovery. Frank
J. Belcher, one of the" richest men of the
Klondike, recently died of the disease,
just as he was about to leave for his
home in Pennsylvania, where he proposed
to retire on a fortune of at least $600,000,
which he had accumulated in the El Do
rado district. A number of other deaths
have resulted from the disease.
A Japanese woman was a victim and
before her death asked, that her body be
shipped south," cremated and the ashes re
turned to her native land. - .
United States mail from Teller City,
within the circle of the arctic; Slnrock,
Nome, St. Michael, and all of the prin
cipal points along the Yukon from, its
mouth to White Horse, arrived to-day on
board the steamer City of Seattle. It is
the third Nome mail received since the
close of the Bering Sea navigation. There
were probably 3000 Nome letters of date as
late as January 15. From the stamping,
the carriers did not leave St. Michael
until eight days subsequent to their de
parture from Nome. Numerous St. Mich
ael letters were stamped January 23.
The Nome mail, which was carried In
a single pouch, contained letters, posted
for every principal city in the United
States and Canada, while not a few. are
addressed to European cities.
The mail reached Skaguay on April 2,
and was aboard the City of Seattle April
4. Within two hours after its arrival at
the Seattle postofllce it was all distrib
uted. . - Â¦â¢

The arrests grew out of the recent Den
ver city elections, In which the Republic
ans, supported by the Denver Republican,
Â¦were victorious. Charges of fraud at the
polls were made by both Republicans and
Democrats, and the newspapers have been
full of the details. The Denver Repub
lican In an editorial published Saturday
morning charged that Senator Patterson
was directly responsible for the crimes
alleged to have been committed by the
Democrats against the ballot; that he was

sonal direction ter Democratic workers on
how to steal the election.
Ine Republican professes to take the ac
tion for libel lightly and intimates that it
is prepared to prove its charges. Eminent
counsel has been retained by both sides,
and the trial is likely to be one ' of the
most sensational ever held - in Denver
Senator Patterson and Mr. Hill are' both
millionaires, and there is long standing
enmity between them. Hill is the son of

DECIDES AGAINST
FRUIT COMBINE
â¢ *
Judge Seawell's Ruling May
Cause Disruption of
Association.
Sustains Defendant's Demurrer in an
the Terms of a Con
. tract
Action Brought to Enforce
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, April 8.-Judpe James M.
Seawell of San Francisco, who recently
heard the suit of the California Cured
Kruit Association against W. Ainswori.li
to compel the latter to deliver to the asso
ciation $700 worth of dried prunes, has
rendered a decision that may affect all of
the association's contracts /with prune
men. At present there are nearly 100
Fimllar suits pending throughout the
State. The opinion of Judge Seawell ap
plies to three cases he recently heard
here, against Ainsworth, F. E. Stelling
and the Phoenix Raisin Seeding and
Packing Company. The local Judges were
disqualified because of being interested.
The decision sustains the demurrer filed
by XV. Ainsworth to the suit of the asso
ciation. The defendant disposed of his
fruit to the Phoenix Dried Fruit Company.
Judge Seawell practically decides that the
association Is entitled to only 2 per cent
of the prunes contracted for. the amount
il was to be given for handling the fruit.
The court holds that there is no allega
tion in the complaint that plaintiff has
sustained damages by reaton of the con
version of the prunes therein mentioned,
or of the amount of euch damages. The
averment that the value of the prunes at
the time of the alleged conversion was
5700 is not the equivalent of an allegation
that the plaintiff has sustained damages
in that amount.-
Overvaluation of Damage.
The amo unt of damages sustained by
plaintiff, by reason of the conversion
complained of, is not necessarily equal to
the value of the prunes. By the action
of the defendants the plaintiff has been
relieved of the labor and expense incident
to the inspection, packing, storage, insur
ance and sale of the prunes, and the obli
gation to account for the price realized
by their sale.
Continuing the court holds:
I flo not think that the provision that upon
failure of Ainsworth to deliver the prunes as
soon as picked and cured plaintiff should be
entitled to assume and take exclusive posses
s'on and control thereof has any greater effect
in conferring title on plaintiff than the prom
ire of Ainsworth to deliver them. Neither was
the agreement to deliver the prunes absolute.
>insworth agreed to cure the prunes to the
satisfaction of plaintiff's inspector, and there
uiwn to deliver the whole thereof to plaintiff.
There is no allegation in the complaint that
the prunes were cured to the satisfaction of
TilalntifTs inspector or were ever inspected by
h-ra. If Ainsworth intended a transfer of the
prunes why was the sale and transfer in terms
limited to 2 per cent? By the contract alleged
l:e sold and transferred 2 per cent absolutely
fir.d merely agreed at a future time and upon
a certain contingency to deliver the whole of
the prunes.
My conclusion is that under the facts 6tated
in the complaint the plaintiff is not entitled
to maintain the action. I cannot take judicial
i.otice of the fact, if it exists, that plaintiff
l:as made a like contract with other fruit
growers, nor do I know judicially the pur
jm- ses fof which plaintiff was org-anized.
It is fair to assume that plaintiff's ability
to carry on its business depends upon the
lidelity with which the persons who contract
with "it carry out their engagements. The
Feme is true of all business undertakings.
Tl'at is, however, a circumstance of no ma
teriality in the construction of the agreement
eel forth in the complaint.
The effect of the decision may be wide
spread and will be watched by the prune
men. The Cured Fruit Association prob
ablv will amend the complaint and make
a hard legal fight. If the decision filed
to-day was to stand, all the dissatisfied
irrowers in the association would break
away.
Directors Decide to Appeal.
The directors of the California Cured
Fruit Association and their attorneys held
a meeting this afternoon and discussed
the results of Judge Seawell's decision.
They were unanimous that the case must
be fought to the bitter end, as the life
of the association depended upon it. An
appeal will be taken to the Supreme
Court.
President Bond said this evening that
the significance of the decision was that
the present contract vrould not hold good
unless the prunes are cut of the growers'
hands and into the warehouse of the as
sociation. While it did not affect the pree
r>nt crop, it would have a destructive ef
fect on next vear's operations and might
disrupt the association. It is the first de
cision ever rendered on the contract used
by most of the co-operative associations.
The Cured Fruit Association's contract
is the same as that of the Raisin Grow
ers' Association, and it will affect that
organization, which now has a similar
Btxu pending. The directors of the asso
ciation and its attorneys are of the opin
ion it will not be sustained. Steps will
be taken to obtain a decision before the
June meeting of the association. If Judge
Seawell should be sustained it means all
contracts with prune men for next year
â¢will be worthless and practically a re
organization of the association must be
had.

RICH PLACER STRIKE
MADE BY PROSPECTORS
Men Rushine'In to Stake Out Claims
TEKOA', April 8.â There is intense ex
citement here over the discovery of rich
placer diggings on Santa Creek, about
twenty miles east of here, in Idaho. Men
left here at all hours last night to stake
claims. Others are rushing in from Parm
ington and other points -In the Palouse.
The discovery was mode by the Tysons
brothers, three farmers, in a gulch which
runs-.into Santa Creek, about eight miles
from the creek's confluence with the St.
Mary. From a. piece of ground 40 by 15
feet they took out $308 in coarse gold.
Their appliances were crude and it is
estimated that, they got less than a third
of the gold, as the -bedrock was not
cleaned at all. The new camp is on the
divide between the old Hoodoo District
and the Coeur d' Alen.es. It is said several
million dollars was taken out of the old
Hoodoo district In early days.
The wreckers sought to derail the heavy
train, with its scores of passengers, and
ditch it in the river, fifty feet below the
track bed. Had they succeeded awful loss
of life would have resulted. A discarded
tie had been placed across the rails, to
which It was bound by ropes, at the north
end of tunnel 5. A few feet? beyond the
obstruction a bridge spans the river for
the support of the track.
Luckily the engineer saw the tie when
his engine was still In the tunnel and
stopped his train within a few feet of the
obstruction that might have hurled more
than 100 persons to their death in the
river. * . .

TUCSON", April 8.â Telegrams were re
ceived to-day from the parents of Laura
Morgan, the sixteen-year-old girl who
disappeared from her home in Coif ax.
Wash., announcing that she was In Tuc
son. A careful search was made for her.
Marshal Taylor was assisted by Deputy
Sheriff McPhall of Tuma. but the girl
succeeded in evading the officers. She
was seen at the Southern Pacific Railroad
yards yesterday afternoon, but when the
officers visited the yards no trace of her
could be found. v Â¦
It is believed that Miss Morgan went to
Phoenix in a freightcar, and Deputy Mc-
Phall has gone thither in search of her.
The girl is traveling as a tramp, beating
her way on the railroads. ;

MEMPHIS," Tenn., April 8.-Before 10,000
persons T. P. Hayes' bay colt Royal Vic
tor won the Tennessee Derby at Mont
gomery Park from John F. Schorr's bay
filly Lady Schorr, with George Long s bay
colt Gaherls third. The time, 1:57, was a
very creditable performance.
The Schorr stable had named three
starters,' but. Alard Scheck and Joe Frey
were scratched and Farmer Bennett was
added to keep Lady Schorr company. Sid
dons, The Commander and Dick Burgess
were also scratched, leaving only contest
ants. The Schorr pait were held at the
prohibitive odds of 1 to 3. while 8 to 1 was
laid against Royal Victor..
After two false breaks they were sent
away to an excellent start, with Royal
Victor a head in front .of Farmer Bennett
and the others close up. Farmer Bennett
and Lady Schorr raced to the front and,'
with Royal Victor a length away, made
the running to the stretch. At this point
Winkfield sent the Hayes colt up to the
leaders, and in the run home he challenged
Lady Schorr. J.. Woods on the latter went
to the whip, but his efforts were of no
avail, Royal Victor, well ridden by Wjnk
fleld, winning handily by three parts of a
length. Gaheris made up a lot of ground
in the stretch and finished th|rd. Results:

says it is impossible to make a statement

with Dleasar.t grounds. No. 56 General
folano street, a fashionable quarter of
the city, beside the Pasig River, which is
being renovated and prepared for occu
pancy.
Aguinaldo if= purchasing diamonds and
r-ther jewelry- He continues to receive
certain visitors, but newspaper corre-
Fnondentp are excluded. It is said that
the manifesto which Aguinaldo has been
preparing has not yet been signed and it
is added that Aguinaldo- is reluctant to
romi-ily with the conditions. It appears
that the majority of the Filipinos in Ma
nila distrust Aguinaldo and dislike to see
him accorded special .favors. They say
he ought to be s-everelv punished.
Gereral Sandico, a former member of
Agulnaldo's cabinet, has surrendered to
the American authorities at Cabanatuan,
!ti the province of Nueva Ecija. He has a
ban 1 record and may be tried.

Fighters Compared,
Oscar Gardner, who is to box Terry Me-
Govern before the National Athletic Club
on the 30th of this month, began training
at Croll's Gardens, Alameda, yesterday,
under the direction of Joe Bernstein.'
Gardner and Bernstein will box and wres
tle and do plenty of road work. Oscar is
confident that he can defeat McGovem.
He claims he was cheated out of a victory
when he met McGovem in New York a
year ago. He said he knocked Terry down
in the first round with a left hook and
the Brooklyn wonder remained down four
teen seconds. Gardner says that when h<?
knocked McGovem off his feet he : was
close to the ropes and in order. to give
McGovem a chance to rise :to his 'feet he
stepped over his prostrate body. - McGbv
ern grabbed his leg and held on until his
brain cleared. Gardner says he was so
disgusted at the treatment accorded him
that he lost all heart and McGovem haJ
no troubl-e putting him away' in three
rounds. Following are the measurements'
c-: the two men taken at the time of their
memorable battle:

HOXOLTJLI". ADril 2.â Robert W. Wll
cox. Hawaii's delegate to Congress, ar
rived home to-day on the transport Han
cock. He is here on- a short visit, intend
ing to return to Washington in May. Wil
cox is exDected. as leader of the Home
Rule party, to bring tQgether the divided

ents hope that his visit here will result

Chinese Consul Yang Wai Pin has in
sued a proclamation, said to be based
upon circulars received from Minister Wu
Ting Fang at Washington, ordering all
members of the Bow Wong "Wui, or Chi
nese Reform Society, to call upon him
and forswear allegiance to the society, on
penalty of having all their relations In
China arrested and thrown into prison if
they refuse. Of the Chinese in the Islands
over S000 are enrolled members of the Bow
Wong Society, and tney are generally dis-:
posed to defy the Consul. Some of them
have already received news of the arrest
of their relatives in China.
The bark Topgallant was sold at auc
tion on the 2Sth of last March, being
knocked down, for $1100. She came here
from Manila after having put in there in
distress from Hqngkong and having been
disabled again after leaving Manila. She
was bought in by her owners, but has
since been sold again, and is now held
by A. L. C. Atkinson and Harry Evans
of this city. She may be refitted and put
in the Laysan Island trade.
The death of a Japanese woman last
Fndnv night is being investigated as a
suspected case of bubonic plague. Her
disease was diagnosed by the attending
physician as typhoid-pneumonia, but post
mortem examinations, together with the
symptoms in. the case, led the Board of
Health to suspect plague. Experiments
are being made with cultures of bacilli
and the result will be known in a few
days.
A general strike of mates of the ves
sels engaged in the interisland trade has
tied up many of the vessels. The mates
employed by the Interisland Steam Navi
gation Company and Wilder Steamship
Company on over twenty vessels have all
struck for higher wages. The men were
only recently given United States certifi
cates to allow them to fill the positions
they occupy and are about all the licensed
mates- here. They want the coast wages
of $90. ?76 and $60 a month for first, sec
ond and third mates. They have been
getting $65,- $40 and $30. Both companies
are holding out. though to-day mo.jt of
their vessels are unable to leave port.

The Pacific Mall Company's steamship
China arrived trom the Orient via Hono
lulu yesterday, one day ahead of time. She
left the islands on the 2d Inst., and at that
time there was no sign of the Oceanic
Steamship Company's Ventura. The Ven
tura was a day late leaving Auckland, N.
7,., ajid probably was further. delayed at
Pago Pago, Samoa, so that she may not
be looked for until this morning. Many of
the passengers booked for her came up on
the China from Honolulu.
The China brought to port, all told, 106
cabin. 00 second cabin and 171 Chinese
passengers. Among her cargo was $583,0(30
la treasure. On March 29. in latitude 28
degrees 'Sit minutes north, longitude 176 de
grees 40 minutes west, the Nicaraguan
bark J. c. Pflufcer, for San Francisco, was
spoken. At midnight April 3 the Oceanic
Steamship Company's Sonoma was spoken
and eight hours later the Occidental and
Oriental Steamship Company's Gaelic was
parsed. The Gaelic sailed from San Fran
cisco March 30 at 1:40 p. m., having a fif
teen hours' start of the Sonoma.

STEAMSHIP MATES ON STRIKE
Trouble on the Vessels of the Hawaii-
an Interisland Fleet.
AVhen the China left Honolulu on the 2d
inst. nearly all the vessels of the Inter
island Steamship Company and the
"Wilder Steamship Company were tied up
for want of officers. The> first, second and
third mates of the two fleets asked for an
advance, in pay, and when the demand
was refused they went out on strike. Men
to till thfir places were advertised for, but
ihe supply of licensed officers Is very lim
ited in the islands, so the steamships had
to tie up.
Before Hawaii became part of the Union
p. mnster mariner did not require a license
to navigate among tho islands. When an
nexation was accomplished and the laws
of the United States ruled all masters and
tnates of steamships had to pass an exam
ination and be licensed by the inspectors
of steam vessels. This threw many men
out of employirent, and sailors with cer
tificates from San Francisco* took their
places. These men now want coast wages.
n;id judging from the news brought by
the China, they are going to get them.
Disinastrd Bark Identified.
The four-masted bark sighted by the
schooner Rosamond is still causing under
writers worry. Every vessel now due on
Hie coast lias been canvassed, and the con
sensus of opinion is that the disabled boat
will probably turn out to be the German
Â¦four-masted bark Willy Rickmers, now
<>ut forty-live days from- Kobe. Japan, for
Portland. She is in ballast and painted
lilack. and so rills the bill as far as it goes.
It will be remembered that the British
bark Willscott was dismasted also while
on her way from Japan to Portland and
put in here under a jury rig.
Otto Gildermeister Again.
The men who gambled on the overdue
Otto Gildcrraeister are not feeling as jubi
lant as tli^y did a month ago, when the
partially dismasted vessel was towed into
Sun Dirgo. There is some talk of her be
ing condemned as a "constructive loss,"
and should this be done the gamblers may
loso their money. The bark is worth about
SKn.<tiyt. and it will cost in the neighborhood
off J-J.n/HXt to repair and refit her. The own
<-rs therefore rray elect to throw the ves
sel on the hands of the underwriters, and
in that event the gamblers may lose.
There is' not more than SD003 involved on
ilio Paeific Coast, and as the reinsurers
have made some big prollts of late they
can nffurd to lose a few dollars now.

of the Supreme Court overruling thj
merit system in the offices mentioned ap
plied to deputies alone. ,' \ '. .
â¢ This decision of the commission mea.is
that the. copyists in the -Recorder's and
County Clerk's offices and the bookkeep
ers and clerks in the 'offices of the Sheriff,
Assessor and Coroner and for the Jus
tice's Clerk ,are regarded as being undbi*
the jurisdiction qf the Civil Service Com
mission. At the meeting of the commis
sion last night no special plan of proced
ure was outlined to bring the matter t;i
an issue. It was agreed, .however, that
should any city officer attempt to remove
a civil service employe other than a dep
uty, except for good and sufficient caus-%
the aid of the courts .would be invoked
to prevent the removal. The action would
probably take the form of a writ of man
damus to ' restrain the appointing powt
from removing such employe until the
status of. the deputies is determined.
President "Freud of the Civil Service
Commission is of the opinion, and in this
he Is backed by Attorney McEnerney,
that it will be no trouble to prove who are
deputies and who are not. The charter
itself provides the number of deputies and
their compensation in each of the offices
affected. Freud says that a deputy is
one who acts for his principal and is com
petent tto administer oaths or signs docu
ments for his superior. â¢ .
The determination of the disputed point
will result in the holding of examinations
for positions outside of deputies in tne
county offices. The commission claims
that the only issue before Judge â¢ Heb
bard was the attempt to -restrain tli2
holding of examinations for deputies. The
opinion affirming the decision of the lower
court is devoted wholly to deputies and
for that reason it is claimed that the
commission has not been enjoined from
holding -examinations lor all other posi
tions. - .:
The commission further decided to go
ahead with the examinations in the mu
nicipal offices not affected by the Supreme
Court decision, beginning with the Boards !
of Works. and Health. The positions in !
the first-named board have all been classi
fied and the first examination will be
held on Thursday, April 25, for offleo
deputies. This will be followed as rapidly
as possible by examinations for superin
tendent of public buildings, superintend
ent of stone pavements, superintendent of
sewers, civil engineers, plumbers, med
sengers, janitors, hydrantmen, stenogra
phers, inspector of light and water, drafts
men, watchmen and other -positions, in
all, 100 examinations will be held. The
commission will endeavor to secure boards
of examiners for each' one, the members
of these boards to serve gratuitously.
It is figured that there are 2000 positions
still subject to the provisions of civil
service, .notwithstanding the Supreme
Court decision. Assessor Dodge states
that he will submit to civil service, -a 1 -
tnough his office is exempted therefrom,
so far as his deputies are. concerned. I--.
was stated that, the copyists will petition
the Supreme Court for a rehearing in or
der to establish their status." It is prob
able, however,; that nothing will be done
in: this connection until, as stated, some
appointing power proceeds to remove a
civil service subordinate.
WORRIED OVER DECISION
OF THE SUPREME COURT
Copyists in Various Departments at
City Hall Anxious as to
Future Action.
The copyists at the City Hall are d2
cidedly uneasy over the recent decision
of the Supreme Court in regard to the
civil service. . "* ' Â¦
If, as is contended, these employes are
no longer to be appointed under the civil
service act, some of them may lose their
positions, hence the anxiety. About forty
civil service appointees are affected by
the decision, and most of them are em
ployed in the County Clerk's and County
Recorder's offces. . .
Neither County Clerk Deane nor Record
er Godchaux are decided as to exactly
what course they will pursue, but several
new men are to be hired in the office of
the latter, and Godchaux states that they
will be copyists whom he will himself
select.
Mr. Godchaux says that his experience
with civil service aupolntees In the past
has not always been pleasant, and that
inefficient employes have been placed in
his office.
-Blanche Partington.
An imperial spectacle IsEmile Berger
afs "More Than Queen," as presented by
Miss Blanche Walsh and her company last
night at the Columbia Theater. "More
Than Queen" is rather a series of splendid
pictures than a play proper, a gallery of
the brilliant scenes in which the Jose
phine episode in the life of the Man of
Destiny played themselves . out, and con
sidered as this, ,. it is a royally sufficient
effort. The prologue shows' the meeting
of the lovely 'Creole; Josephine Beauhar
nals, and Napoleon In the srarden of tha
Palais Royal, an admirably beautiful pic
ture. The first act takes place . at the
Chanterine residence of the Bonaparte3,
in 1799. Napoleon has gust returned from
Egypt and discovers Josephine about to
attend one of the masked balls so much
in vogue during the. period. The threads
of the plot, such plot as there is. here
begin to discover I themselves. Josephine
is childless, it is the wish of the First.
Consul to become an Emperor, and it is
necessary for the stability of the empire
that there be an heir to the throne. There,
is only one' way for the Emperor to take,
to divorce the faithful woman who has
been all to him but the mother of his
children, and it is the story of the sacri
fice of the Empress that is told by M.
Bergerat:
Napoleon has announced his return from
Egypt to the Bonaparte sisters, not to
his wife. She discovers this and attends
the ball on the evening of his return.
He, misled by lying reports from his in
terested relatives, believes. her faithless
She returns from the ball, and in an ex
quisitely dainty scene compels the sulky
general to capitulate.
The- garden of Malmaison, favorite
home of Josephine, Is the scene of the
second act and shows the coming -of 'the
envoys to the First Consul with the offer
of imperial honors. The third act, scene
first, takes place in the palace of the
Tuileries, showing the events of the morn
ing of the coronation of Napoleon and
the jealousy of the Bonaparte sisters of
the new-made Empress. The second
scene is the coronation in the Church of
Notre Dame, and is a picture that for
magnificence has seldom been equaled on
the local stage. 'Act IV opens â¢ in the
Palace of St. Cloud and is the one dram
atic scene in the play. The Palace of
Fontainebleau, in the last act, shows the
resignation of the Empress to the will of
her husband in the signing of the divorce.
Orpheum.
' As the Empress, Miss Walsh adds much
to her worthy reputation. Gracious, arch,
queenly, tender, womanly, she makes all
there i3 to be made of the part. Her
gowns are splendid and beautiful, she
looks the Empress every inch and won the
audience at once with her work. She is
well â fairly well â supported. Napoleon is
essayed by William Humphrey, at first
apparently rather a lightweight repre
sentative of the Corslcan genius.- He
warmed up to his work later in the even
ing, however, and in the big fourth act he
became for the time the vain and splendid
Man of Destiny. Talleyrand, the "most
good-natured man that ever cut a throat,"
is played in a guileless fashion by Ogden
Stevens. Lucien Bonaparte is well done
by Robert Lowe, and the part of Roustan,
the Mameluke, affords Frank Sheridan
opportunity for a brilliant bit of dramatic
recital that begins the good things in the
one good act of the play. Elizabeth May
hew and Helen Singer 'take good care of
the parts of the wicked sisters, and the
rest of the cast is acceptably filled.
"More Than Queen" is worth the money
for the settings alone. Buhl cabinets,
tapestry, carvings, pictures, all historical
ly correct and exquisitely artistic; out
door scenes of rare beauty and costumes
of admirable taste and splendor, all leave
an impression oÂ£ unusual beauty. The play
will remain for two weeks at the Colum
bia. . \
The splendid bill presented by the Or
pheum. management last night made a de
cided hit with the big audience. The Pre
vosts do an acrobatic turn that keeps
the spectators gasping with wonder.
Josephine Gassman is as clever as ever,
and scored an instantaneous hit with her
pickaninnies, who are .wonderfully cute
and amusing. The droll stories of Wood,
the monologist, reached every -one and hn
was given an enthusiastic welcome.
Stephens, in his imitations of Joseoh Jef
ferson. Henry Irving and other" great
footlight stars, showed great ability, an.l
is a feature of the bill. "The Girl of
Quality," Hamilton Hill and Tschernoff
and his dogs, holdovers, were warmly
greeted, and the great American biograph
was as popular as ever, j
Tivoli.
The "standing-room-only" sign of la.?t
season is once again displayed at the
popular home of comic opera and crowds
are flocking to the Tivoli to witness the
delightful revival of "The Idol's Eye" i:i
all its wealth of music and scenic effects
Needless to say that Ferris Hartman and
Alf C. Wheelan are as amusing as ever,
while Cunning-ham, the new barytone, had
leaped into instant favor. Maud Williams,
Annie Meyers, Bernice Holmes and Ed
ward Webb make up an unrivaled cast.
As for the chorus, the young ladies are
beautiful, to look upon and know how to
sing. The orchestra is en rapport with
th3 singers and the entire production is
so pleasing. that a chancre r.t programmo
is not to be looked for for some weeks.
Alcazar.
"Tennessee's Pardner" commenced its
secondand last week at the Alcazar
Theater last night before a crowded
house. There were several, curtain calls
and the applause was unstinted. Frank
Bacon's portrayal of the part of Gewilll
ker Hay is one of the features of the play.
The singing by the New Century quartet
of "The Days pf '49" was warmly ap
plauded. There should be large audiences
during the best of the week.
Central.
â¢ "Ingomar," as Marie Lovell paints him,
is a barbarian of the mountains with an
inherited love of freedom and his neigh
bor's goodsâ a rough, bass-voiced, fearles3
leader of men as brave and as fearless aa
he. So Robert" Downing, 'rough, bass
voiced and forestian in virility, paints him
on the mimic stage of the Central Thea
ter. Â¦ The scoffing humor of the uncul
tured nomad, who found in weakness and
tears subject for laughter, who lived on
stolen cattle and the booty of the boM
foray, yet who became a willing slave
to* a pretty woman's smile and who re
pelled with hot indignation the offer to
heap honors upon him and to give him the
girl he loved as the reward for the pro
posed betrayal of his comrades in arms
and robbery, is faithfully and consistently
portrayed by Mr. Downing in the beauti
ful play. Strength is his predominating
â¢characteristic and strength of -voice .Â¦ami
movement without apparent effort.'. WhilÂ»
differing in method, and perhaps lacking
in the subtle dramatic discrimination that
made Edwin Forrest great, Mr. Downing
follows closely, in the wake of the sten
torian, tragedian in the role of Ingomar.
He is ably supported by Lorena Atwood^
who portrays the character of Parthenla
in an unexpectedly artistic way. .
: Robert Cummings as Alastor fulfilled
the expectation of the public, who had
seen his performance in the "Gladiator "
The play is well staged and costumed and
will run the remainder of the week to full
houses. â¢ ;,

It happens, however, that we have information upon
that phase of government in the island from another
source. The New" York Post recently published &n
account of the Porto Rican courts given by W. T.I
Abbott, who is described as a lawyer of eight years'
standing, and who" for eighteen months has been a
resident of Porto Rico. According to that statement
the insular courts are ignorant arid corrupt, and the
Judges, "decide entirely in the interest of the party
who brings most influence to bear either in money or
otherwise."
Concerning the fitness of the native office-holders
the Governor does not. say much. That would seem
to be a subject which the Governor thinks should not
be discussed just now. The islanders are not used to
American methods 'of procedure and will have to
learn. We must not expect too much at the start.
Concerning the Government itself the Governor
sayp the administration has desired and has sought to
obtain the co-operaticn of all good citizens. It is
required of those who aspire' to insular offices that
they "be loyal to the American Government, and that
fitness for office with such loyalty be the fundamental
requirement rather than the indorsement of some
political leader."
â¢-Â» OVERNOR ALLEN of Porto Rico, who has
f "~i just gone to Washington for the purpose of
' consulting with the President, gives a good ac
count of affairs in the island and describes the condi
tions of the people as most promising. They appear,
to have made marked progress, not only in the various
lines, of industry, -but in those of civil government;
and the prospects are that we shall not have any seri
ous problems to deal with in governing the country.
It is, of course, to be expected that the Governor
would make a favorable report of what has been. done
urder his own. direction, and something must be
allowed for the inevitable bias in the mind of a Gov
ernor speaking of the results of his own work. When
such allowance has been made, however, there remains
enough in the Governor's statement to justify the
hopeful view he takes of the situation. He is able to
say: "The civil government established in Porto Rico
is working smoothly. The various departments have
been organized and our administration is seemingly
giving general satisfaction. In a material way thers
has been wonderful progress. Most of the land hot
under cultivation under the old regime has been taken
up and made profitable. * * * In the old estab
lished industries there has been great improvement
in all except coffee growing and production."

whistle. However, another* time, when necessity for
stamp duties arises, Congress will be on its guard.
Yet these tax shirkers have deliberately refused to
bear their share of the burden of this war, and on the
contrary have employed tricky lawyers throughout the
cour.tr>' to utilize the processes of the courts in stand
ing off litigation designe.l to make them perform their
duty. . It is a safe estimate to say that Wells, Fargo &
Co. has profited in this State by its contumacious
ness to the extent of a quarter of a million dollars.
A meaner and more unpatriotic position was never
Uken by a grasping corporation than this. These
stamps were issued to pay for maintaining a war
against a foreign power, through which the United
States has acquired a vast area of territory, over which
in due time the express companies of the country will
extend their lines, and from which they will derive
revenue.
But the moral obligation of paying these stamp
taj.es still rests upon Wells, Fargo & Co. and the
ether unpatriotic corporations which united with it
after the passage of the law in open defiance of tlie
Government. It is estimated that in. this State alone
Wells, Fargo & Co. kns collected from its customers
between $8odb and $10,000 per month in stamp taxes.
Rates were not increased to cover this amount, jbut
the corporation insolently defied the law and refused
to perform its duty as a common carrier unless its
customers paid for the stamps.

Capital seeking investment, the induce
ment to engage in enterprises which will
meet wants in the home country, the de
sire to settle their emigrants where neith
er their labor nor their allegiance will be
lost to the fatherlandâ these are the eco
nomic and social grounds on which may
be explained the desire of the Germans
to colonize; where such, economic, and so
cial relationships are established it Is but
a step to political control, and the politi

Noteâ Next - Tuesday will be published
a paper by Dr. George on "Germany's Co
lonial Policy." .
The bearing of this great change upon
the subject of German colonization is two
fold. In the first place, her manufactures
have progressed so rapidly that Germany
is not only able to supply her own needs
in a great many lines but is obliged to
go out into the world to seek new mar
kets. German savings, too, have increased
her capital until It no longer finds suffi
cient employment at home, and is seek
ing investment in new fields. In the
second place, the agricultural produce of
the country no longer suffices to support
Its ever-increasing populationâ a fact
which can better -be appreciated Â¦ if we
Imagine 80 per cent of our own popula
tion compressed within the one State of
Texas and there forced to earn a living,
for Germany is not as. large even as Texas
would be minus the area of the State of
Illinois, while her population is fast ap
proaching the 60,000,000 mark.
Foodstuffs Wanter; Goods to Sell.
A direct result of this change has been
a great increase In population, notwith
standing the continued heavy drain
through emigration, for a nation which
has developed industries as well as agri
culture is capable of sustaining a larger
population than a purely agricultural na
tion. During the quarter century from
1S25 to 1S60 the increase In population was
considerably less than 7.000.000, while dur
ing the quarter century from 1870 to 1S93
the increase was from. 40.S16.000 to 52,247.
000. or nearly 11,500,000, and that, too, in
fact of the fact that during these years
the country lost more heavily through
emigration than in any other period of its
history. That this increase in population
is directly connected with the growth of
tha great manufacturing Industries may
be seen from the changed ratio of rural
to urban population. From 1S71 to 1S90
there was an actual decrease of nearly
35.000 in the number of people in the agri
cultural districts, while in the same pe
riod the population in the cities and towns
increased" nearly 8,500,000. In 1S95 only 38
per cent of the population were engaged
In agriculture, while 39 per cent were en
gaged in the great industries of the cities
and in mining and 11 per cent In com
merce and transportation.
The political change brought about by
the establishment of the empire was an
other stride in advance. It had to do not
only with. the relations of the German
states among themselves but also the pro
motion in a political way of the commer
cial interests of the united people and the
continued allegiance to the f aCtherland of
the thousands of emigrants who: were
leaving and settling, in distant countries:
During the fifty years from 1820 to 1870
The Tide of Emigration.
With the founding of the empire Ger
many broke with the past and entered
politically and economically upon a new
era. These political and economic
changes, together with the emigration
from Germany of large numbers of her
people, are the prime factors in her pres
ent colonial policy. The political change
bi ought about by the founding of the em
pire was in some respects the comple
ment of the change brought about forty
years earlier by the establishment of the
Commercial League (zollverein). This
league broke down tariffs and other bar
riers? to trade which had existed from
time immemorial between the various
German states, and prepared the way for
the great industrial changes of more re
cent date. It established free trade
ai- ong- 26,000,000 Germansâ the first great
step toward national unity. By creating
a . community of interests on commercial
and trading questions it prepared the way
for political unity; by overcoming local
prejudice and habit It created a stronger
feeling of German nationality. Â¦
Impulses Due to National Unity.
sclia . empire. There had been frequent
attempts at colonization by. German ad
venturers during earlier periods. Ger
man settlements existed on the coast or
Africa more than 200 years ago.Â« Still ear
lior. ships from BrandenbwrK found their
way to the west coast of Africa In quest
of slaves and gold, and Brandenburgers.
together with the Prussian company of
Emden, finally managed at the close of
the seventeenth centuhy to establish a
foothold on the Gold Coast. As early as
16S1 the Brandenburg merchants had -or
ganised for purposes of trade in Africa
the Brandenburg African Company, but
by 1720, distracted by quarrels at home,
it had abandoned its West African enter
prise. Â¦ Asain, in the early *40s of the
fast century, Germany gave some consid
eration to the question of colonization,
this time to the unoccupied territories In
America. From 1S60 to 1S65 her attention
was again turned to Africa, when ft Han
overian baron. Von der Decken, was ex
plcrlng the East African coast. It oc
curred to him that Zanzibar and the ad
jacent mainland would be a good field for
German colonization and enterprise, es
pecially as the opening of the Suez canal
would soon shorten the route to those re
gions. His idea'was acted upon, and the
work begun by him was carried; on by
Hamburg merchants, who established a
nourishing trade at Zanzibar.
This measure is so much of an innovation upon
existing methods of vutirig in this country that its
passage is not likely; but it has had the effect of rous
ing a good deal of discussion on the subject, and in
the end something â irsy be done to put an end to
minority rule. It appears that in New York, at. any
rale, the' "third party" has become a political nuisance.
A still more curious proposal in the way of abolish
ing old customs is before the New Yorkers. There has
been introduced into the State Senate a bill entitled,
"An act to diminish the loss^of votes on third party
candidates and to promote election by majority." The
bill provides for permitting the voter to specify a
second, choice on his billot, so that if the first choice
be defeated the second choice will count. :Â¦ It is argued,
by way of example, that when the good citizens of
New York city were divided in their votes between
Seth Low and General Tracy, thus permitting the
Tammany candidate to become elected by a minority
of the people, they would have been able to elect either
Low or Tracy had they been permitted to indicate
their second choice. %
The bill is regarded cs a virtual abolition of capital
punishment, because it is believed a jury would never,
except in very atrocious cases, recommend the death
penalty, and a Judge would h'ardly set aside a solemn
expression of a jury in favor of the lighter punishment.
The enactment of the measure would thus by indirec
tion accomplish nearly. a)l that the opponents of cap
ital punishment desire. Strangely enough, the matter
lias not attracted much attention. The bill was passed
in the Assembly by a vote of 94 to 26, and we learn
from our New York exchanges that the passage came
as a surprise to the public, as few people were aware
that any such measure was under consideration.
Another bill affecting punishment for crime passed
the New York Assembly some days ago and is re
ported to have a fair chance of enactment. The meas
ure has been described as a bill to abolish capital pun
ishment. It provides that murder in the first degree
shall be punishable by death or by imprisonment for
life, and murder in the second degree by imprisonment
for life or for a term of not less than thirty years, and
the jury in every case is required to accompany its
verdict with a recommendation to the court stating its
preference as to the alternative penalties, the final
choice between them resting with the trial Judge.
It is said one of the effects of the law will be the
release under parole of the notorious Younger broth
ers, whose criminal exploits were once the sensation
of the time and are not yet wholly forgotten. The
Youngers have been in prison'for twenty-four years,
and, with the allowance of one-third time for good
conduct, which they h?ve under the Minnesota law,
and which they are said to have earned, they are now
.entitled to parole.
The Minnesota Legislature has virtually- abolished
life-imprisonment by; the enactment of a parole prison
law, which provides: "No convict who is serving
under life sentence shall be paroled until he shall have
ser.ved under such sentence thirty-five years, less the
diminution which he would have been allowed by law
for good conduct had he been sentenced fo^ a. term of
thirty-five years."
I N addition to their distinguished efforts in the way
I cf enacting new reforms by statute, several of the
* Eastern Legislatures have ma'de notable efforts at
re form, not by making more laws, but by abolishing
or amending old ones. Some of these efforts are de
serving of general attention. We . may not care to
follow the exainple set in the East, but it will be inter
esting, at least, to note what the Solons of that section
are doing.
ABOLISHING OLD LAWS
The modern movement for colonization
in Germany has taken place entirely
within the last quarter of .the century
just past, and is a result, to a very large
extmt, of the unification in 1871 of the
various independent German states into a
Early Colonization by Germans.
Of the fourteen' nations controlling the
colonies, protectorates and dependencies
of the world, Germany stands third as
regards the area of the territory subject
to her control, and fourth as regards
population. In both respects Great Brit
ain leads, the total area of her posses
sions of this character being . 11,187,000
square miles, and their population J 356,
781,000. France comes next with an'area
of 3,304,000 square miles and a total pop
ulation of 50,372,000. Third in .respect to
population come the colonies of tho Neth
eilands, with 31,717,000 in an area of but
630,000 square miles; while Germany's col
onies and spheres of . Influence comprise
an area of 1,025,000 square miles and a
population of over 11,000,000. But, while
Germany stands, third as regards area
and fourth as regards population, it must
not be forgotten that she was the last
to enter the field of colonization, having
gained political control of all her colonial
possessions since 1884.
'I^ut in the meantime there have been
other considerations bearing in the same
direction. During the last quarter cen
tury there has been going on in Germany
an industrial revolution not unlike the In
dustrial revolution which took place in
England at an earlier period: from beins
chiefly an agricultural nation with small
Industries Germany has been becoming:
more and more a manufacturing nation.
Germany's Industrial Revolution.
The great mass of these people came to
the United States, though considerable
numbers went to Australia, South "Amer
ica and the Cat>e. This great movement
of population from Germany was a heavy
loss from a national point of view. More
over,, in the ease of those who went to
English colonies, Germany- not only felt
the loss to herself but regarded tne gain
to England, her political and social rival,
with great jealousy. To find new terri
tory to which these emigrants could be
turned and to maintain in them a spirit
of nationality and allegiance to their na
tive land was the first great motive lead
ing to colonization. _v
it is estimated that about 3.500,000 persons
emigrated from Germany, while during
the comparatively short period of twenty
years, from 1S71 to 1S91â the period when
the new colonization movement was fast
taking holdâ nearly 2.000.000 of the popula
tion left to improve their- condition in
newer countries.

If you have discovered a new method of
budding trees that has noi been used by
any one else before, you probably could
claim the discovery of a new and 'useful
art and apply for a patent thereon. The
patent law of the United States provides
that patents are to be issued in the name
of the United States, and under the seal
of the Patent Office* to any person. wh.o
has invented or discovered any new and
useful art, machine, manufacture, or com
position of matter or any new and useful
improvement thereof, not known or used
by others in this country before his in
vention or discovery thereof and not pat
ented or described in any printed publica
tion in this or any foreign country, befors
his Invention or discovery thereof or more
than two years prior to his application,
and not in public use or on sale in the
United States for more than two years
prior to his application, unless the same
is proved, to have been abandoned; and
by any person who, by his own industry
genius, efforts and expense, has invented
and produced any new and original de
sign for a manufacture, bust, statue, alto
relievo, or bas-relief; any new and or
iginal' design for the printing of woolen
cotton, or other fabrics; any new
and original Impression, ornament, pat
tern; print, or picture to be printed, paint
ed, cast, or otherwise placed on or worked
into any article of manufacture; or any
new, useful, and original shape or config
uration of any article of manufacture, the'
same not having been known nor used by
others before his invention or production
thereof, nor patented nor described In any
printed publication, upon payment of the
fees required by law and other due pro
ceedings had. Â« v

DISPLACEMENTâ Subscriber, South
San Francisco, Cal. Displacement ton
nage as applied to ships is found in the
same manner as register tonnage except
that measurements are made, not along
and from the tonnage deck, but from the
load water line and the final cubic con
tent Is divided by S3. Displacement is the*
quantity of liquid which Id displaced by a
solid body placed In It. If the weight ot
the displacement Is greater than or equal
to that of the body, the latter will float.
If less, It will sink to tho bottom. A
buoyant material sinks to a level where
the pressure of a liquid displaced Is suf
ficient to counterbalance Its weight.

SIXTEEN TO ONE-G. S.. City. The
following definition of 16 to 1 is takea
from United States Treasury Circular No.
123: The phrase "15 to 1," as applied to
coinage, means that the mint value of
sixteen ounces of silver shall be equal to
the mint value of one ounce of gold: that
Is. that sixteen ounces of silver shall be
coined Into a3 many silver dollars as ono
ounce of gold is comable into standard
dollars.
DEBATESâ Subscriber, City. This dey
partment has on a number of occasions
announced that it does not furnish rea
sons to be used on the positive or nep
tive side of a debate, for the reason that
If such reasons are furnished they will be
those of the writer and not those of the
debater. This department suggests that
you go to the Free Public Library period
ical room, read up for and against the
subject matter you desire to debate on.
and then form your upinion.

ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
Considering the whole subject it may be said Porto
Rico is in good condition, and is in a fair, way to
make rapid progress. Up to this time, therefore, we
have no reason to regret that we annexed the island,
nor have the islanders an> cause, for complaint against
the manner or the meaning of our Government.
over a valuable ' watc-powcr privilege, f it developed
that two of the five Judges had been retained as coun
sel on one side of the case and had given opinions,
and two on the other side, and under those circum
stances it is hot difficult to understand how the de
cision of the presiding Justice was to be given. Now,
that is not regarded in Porto Rico as anything out of
the way' It is According to custom which has'been
in daily practice there always."
Upon that showing it is clearly the duty , of the
United States to establish honest '! courts as soon as
possible. That appeals to be -the chief need of the
island just now. The. natives seen to be peaceable
enough. Governor Allen says: "Some disturbances
hnve occurred, but never with such serious results as
have often occurred in the United States barroom
brawls. Hooting and howling crowds on the streets,
a" few stones thrown, some rattling of sticks along
the palings have been sufficient 10 occasion sensa
tional cables, to the north, which, though promptly
denied, have been circulated to the discredit of the
island." '" IV^.1
The law of liberty, the universal statute of indepen
dence and self-government, is written in the hearts of
such people in Cuba. Such hearts have always been
the tablets which have borne its inscription. If we are
guing'to break faith and lie to the world in the Cuban
settlement, as we have been lied to either three years
ago or now, let us do it like bold buccaneers, declaring
that we propose to take the island and exploit it be
cause we want it in bur business, and disregard the
arpiration of the people for liberty. The pirate sleeps
sweeter over his spoil, rocked in the cradle of the
deep, than the hypocrite who sinks to rest after even
ing prayers.
It has been so always. The English revolution which
destroyed the royal prerogative was led by a brewer
and was fought out by :i Jot of psalm-singing cartmen.
Masinello was a Neapolitan fisherman, Schamyl was a
piountaineer, and Ypsilanti was a vine dresser.
The future deliverer of Russia will probably be the
grandson of a serf, and certainly not of a bayard.
try and allies of Great Britain.
The record of man's aspiration for liberty and inde
pendence is not the biography of "the best classes," as
now understood. American independence was aspired
to by Thomas Paine, the staymaker; Franklin, the
printer; Knox, "the stationer's clerk; Putnam, the
farmer, who plowed his stony fields with oxen and
cculd hardly write his name, and by John StarK,
a clodhopper; Nathan Hale, a poverty-smitten
teacher; Ethan Allen, a rude woodsman; and the plain
people endured and suffered for liberty, while the
best people who shared their lot could be counted on
the fingers. Those of culiure'and refinement, of wealth
aiid respectability, were Tories, enemies of their coun-
In behalf of our own countrymen and the national
honor, we protest against thisjiew installment of offi
cial information and views.
Turning to the Congiessional reports and speeches
of three years ago we find that the "wealth and cul
ture," the best and most respectable people of Cuba,
were therein denounced as sordid supporters of Spain,
while the rest of the people, now denounced as preda
tory riffraff, were gasping on the point of a spear and
dying for : liberty. Those who survived are now
denounced as being for independence merely to get
office, a motive that must be abhorrent to every Amer
ican politician.
A Democratic newspaper, which plays Jekyll and
Hyde with, the administration, admits that these Con
gressional views are in notable contrast to the Con-" 1
gressionar views of three ' years ago. The official
pilgrims then saw through their emotions and found
heroes, warriors and patriots. But the present "en
lightened and honorable members" find "the respect
able classes" desirous vi annexation and opposed to
independence, while the rest of the people are a lot of
predatory riffraff, who have none of the culture and
respectability of the island with them.
One of them says he doubts the safety, of capital in
Cuba, and he finds the best people do not want inde
pendence, and that those who do are hot the best peo
ple and are merely looking for office. Another says
the people are not fit nor ready for self-government.
Another distinguished Senator returns and hastens
to say that it is only a matter of time when the island
will be annexed, and that the present constitutional
convention is not a 1 epresentative body. The best
people want annexation, as do a vast majority of the-
American people. .'Â¦ Â¦ :,
Congress adjourned on March 4. In that brief time
parties of Congressmen have visited Porto Rico and
Cuba and have, returned wise in knowledge of ths
whole situation. *. ; ;
If the kind of information that is being furnished by
Congressmen now had been furnished then, there
would have, been no war, and if the kind we had then
were. furnished now, there would not be ahead of us
the prospect of more trouble. If the truth is told by
Congressmen now/the Congressmen then did not tell
the truth. ' .:
CONGRESSMEN ON CUBA.
ARE we not in danger of a slight surfeit of Con
gressional information and views about Cuba?
The country had a deal of the same sort before
the Spanish war. It was the means of drawing us into
thst enterprise and of launching us upon the sea of
troubles whereon we arc now drifting with no sails to
speak of, rudder gone, compass magnetized, and chro
nometer out of order.
(COPTRIGHT, 1901.)

Dairy Is Unionized.
Through an error in the columns of yes
terday's Call the statement was made that
the National Dairy, which is owned bv
the Barreilles brothers, is the only insti
tution of that kind which does not employ
union labor. The Labor Council states
that the dairy is thoroughly unionized and
that all the men employed by it are mem
bers of the union.
Frank Botsford, agent of the Equitable
Life Association, who was held to answer
before the Superior Court about a week
ago by Judge Fritz on a charge of felony
embezzlement and was released on J100J
bonds, was arrested yesterday afternoon
on Market street by Detective JFitzgerald
on the bench warrant issued by Judge
Fritz Saturday. The Judge had been in
formed that Botsford intended to jump
his bonds, but this Botsford denies. His
bond will probably be increased.

CARELESS CHINESE â¢
INJURES A VETERAN
Euns Him Down With a Laundry
"Wagon at the Geary and Kearny â¢
Street Crossing.
William Mullins, a veteran of the Mex
ican war, was knocked down and seriously
injured last night at the crossing of
Geary and Kearny streets. . .
Mullins, who resides at 443 Eddy street,
was crossing Kearny street when a wagon
driven by Sing Yick, a Chinese laundry
man, came upon him. Before the old man
could get out of the way he was thrown
heavily to the pavement. He was picked
up in an unconscious condition and car
ried to the sidewalk. An ambulance was
sent for and the old man was taken to
the Central Emergency Hospital. An ex
amination showed he had received a
badly shattered kneecap and several lacer
ations of the scalp. His wounds were
dressed and the injured man was made as
comfortable as Dossible.
It is feared that he has received internal
Injuries. Mullins is 76 years old. On ac
count of his age his injuries might result
fatally.
Policeman Behan arrested the Chinaman
and charged him with battery.
THE California Club's exhibition of
the industrial and allied arts
opens to-morrow evening with a
display of the arts and crafts of
California fuller and more repre
sentative than any ever before _ held in
San Francisco. .
The exhibitors have been charged neith
er entrance fee nor commissions on sales.
The small admission fee charged to the
public is for the purpose of reimbursing
the club for the heavy expense of the ten
days' exhibitâ 25c for single admission anrl
$150 for season tickets, good for twenty
admissions and : transferable, are tti'j
rates. . Â¦ * Â¦
The exhibit in the art gallery of tfie
Pavilion is to be open only afternoons and
evenings, beginning at 12 o'clock noon.
Entertaining programmes are prepared
for each day and evening. For tue
opening to-morrow evening the pro
gramme is in charge of Mrs. Edward
X. Rolker of the committee on textiles.
Following are the attractions offered,
Donald Y. Campbell being master of cere
monies:
Overture, orchestra; formal opening of the
exposition by Hon. James D. Phelan, Mayor of
San Francisco; selected songs, Mrs. Gustavus
M. Arnold; speech. Mrs. Katherlne M. Ball,
representing- the art section of the California
Club; barytone solo, "The Gypsy King" (Tre
borne), Carl Yoho; address, "The Art Resources
of California." Hon. D. M. Delmas; solo- from
"Cavalleria Rusticana" (Mascagnl), Mrs. Gus
tavus M. Arnold, accompanied by Fred Maurer.
In the section of the gallery devoted to
textiles the attractions are many.; Ex
quisite modern laces, vieing with those of
the olden time, designed and wrought by
Mrs. Clyde Payne, form her loan exhibit.
HIGH JINKS FOR THE .
NATIVE SON DELEGATES
Those Going to the Grand Parlor to
Be Entertained by Past
Presidents.
The delegates from the several parlor?
of the Native Sons will, in a short time,
assemble In this city on their way to tin-
Grand Parlor to meet in Santa Barbara.
In the past Stanford Parlor has annually
entertained the delegates just before the
session, but this year the Past Presi
dents' Association will assume that func
tion. At a meeting of that association
last night it was decided to secure Cali
fornia Hall in which to hold high jinks
on the night of the 20th lnst., and D. Q.
Troy, T. C. Cornmy, J. Emmett Hayden,
J. A. Steinbach and Andrew Mocker were
appointed a committee to make arrange
ments.
There will be a high-class programme,
many of the numbers of which will be
contributed by professional talent from
the local theaters. Henry S. Martin wiM
be tendered the position of sire for the
night.

No Lights Showing Exits.
A warrant was secured from Judge Me
gan yesterday by Polieeman Cassidy for
the arrest of Nicholas Dethlef serf, pro
prietor of? a lodging-house at 644 Howard
street, for violating the fire ordinance.
There was a -small fire in the lodging
house Saturday night, and it was noticed
by the â¢ firemen that there were no red
lights showing the exits or fire escapes as
required by the ordinance. Cassidy said
that Dethlefsen - had been frequently
warned to ; place the > lights i in position,
but, had neglected to do so.
SOME TESTIMONY TAKEN .
IN GLAZE MUBBEB CASE
Twelfth- Juror Selected and Prosecu
tion Introduces Diagram of
* Scene of Tragedy.
The jury was secured yesterday for. the
trial of. Robert E. Glaze, who is charged
with having murdered his partner,- Wil
liam Trewhella, at .the Windsor Hotel
on | January 14 last; .
When the. court was adiourned.on Fri
day by Judge . Dunne, eleven jurors had
been secured and yesterday the twelfth
was added . to the list, .with but little de
lay, Donald McDonald, a wholesale
liquor dealer, 120 Front street, was the
man selected to- complete the jury.
After the reading of the indictment by
Clerk Mahony, the prosecution . called Po
lice Officer ' George W. . Russell, who had
prepared plans of the scene of the trag
edy. Russell consumed an-' hour in 'ex
plaining to the jury the diagram of the
hotel and the location of the kitchen
stairs, near where Glaze Is alleged to
have waited and shot .Trewhella to death.
Owing to Attorney Foote having an en
gagement during the afternoon, the hear
ing of testimony was adjourned - at the
close of Â¦ the morning session, until this
morning at 10. The jurors will be taken
to the scene of the shooting in order that
they can become familiar with the main
points of the testimony.
