APFOINTrNG CANVASSERS
TO COLLECT THE FUNDS
Twenty-Five Thousand Dollars Need-
ed to Pay Expense of Entertain
ing the President.
F. W. Dohrmann. chairman of the
finance committee for the reception to
President McKinley. presided at a meet
ing of citizens in the rooms of the San
Francisco Board of Trade yesterday after
noon, at which the matter of raising funds
was discussod and a number of sub-com
tuittecs appointed to canvass the city.
Mr. Dohrmann announced that $25,000
would be required for the entertainment
of the distinguished visitors, including ho
tel expenses, carriages and the decora
tion of Market street. Concerning the lat
ter, he said it was hoped that the electric
lighting arrangements, so far as the pub
lic buildings were concerned, would be of
a permanent character.
The work of appointing- eommitteemen
to canvass the forty-seven districts into
w hich the city has been divided was then
taken up and will be completed at an
other meeting which will be held this aft
ernoon in the rooms of the Merchants' As
sociation, when it Is expected a complete
list of the canvassing committees will be
given out.
KLING THRASHED -FOR
KICKING LUCY'S BOY
Interesting Fight Between a Saloon-
Keeper and Teamster Is De
scribed in' Court.
James Lucy, saloon-keeper c.t D-lo Bryant
street, appeared before Judge Cabaniss
yesterday on a charge of battery. The
complaining witness was John Kling, ?i
teamster, living at 48s Eighth street.
After. hearing the evidence the Judge dis
missed the case.
It appeared that on March 29 Lucy's
boy Denis was playing around the stables
at Bryant and Chesley streets, wnere
Kling is employed. Kliug kicked the boy,
who in running away from him struck
his head against . a , .truck and nearly
knocked his right eye out. Two days later
Lucy went to the stables and asked for
the driver "of the grey team." Kling said
he was the man. "You are the man who
kicked my boy," said- Lucy, and pitched
into him. ' Kling had a curry Comb In hli
hand and cut Lucy several times on the
face with it, but he came out second best
in the encounter, as Lucy at one time was
classed as a leading lightweight pugilist.
Kling said: "He put it all over me."
The boy was in court and told the Judge
how Kling had kicked him, and how he
got the blow on the eye. The Judge
thought that Lucy could not be blamed
in the circumstances. . Â¦
Lucy's boy had sworn to a warrant for
Kling's arrest in Judge Fritz' court, but
when the case was called yesterday, Lucy
said he did not want to prosecute Kllngr,
as he was a workinerman, and he was
satisfied he had been taught a lesson he
would not forget. The case was dismlssed-
MINIATURE CANNON IN
BED OF RARE ROSES
Magnificent Dinner Given in Honor
â¢ of the Kessler-Cunningham
Bridal Party. .,
The ladies and gentiemen who are to
act as bridesmaids and ushers at the
Kessler-Cunningham wedding on Wednes
day evening were the guests of honor at
a delightful dinner piven last evening: by
Mrs. Cunningham at her home on Clay
street. It was without exception quite the
prettiest dinner of the season. The table
was uniquely decorated, red, the arSlllery
color, predominating. * In the center of
the table was a miniature cannon sur
rounded by a bed of deep red roses, and
at either end of the table loaded car
tridges peeped out from beneath immense
bunches of Papa Gontier blossoms. Flags/
draped the walls of the dining-room and'
tiny silk banners were placed at intervals
on the board, adding much to the effect
iveness, of the decorations.
At the plate of each^lady of the bridal
party was placed a heart-shaped red jew
elry case, containing a pin fashioned like
a four-leaf clover, in the heart of which
glistened a tiny brilliant. These pins were
Miss Cunningham's souvenir gifts to her
maids of honor and bridesmaids. '
Seated- at table were Miss Charlotte
Cunningham, Miss Charlotte Ellinwood,
Miss Leontine Blakeman. Miss Mary Pol
hemus, Miss Kate Mav . Dillon, Miss May
Reiss, Miss Lillie Reiss, Miss Jean Hos
kins, Miss Mattie Pratt. Miss Mattie
Steele Milton, Major Boyd, Captain Eu
gene T. Wilson, Lieutenant Guy Carlton,
Lieutenant William A. Lieber, Lieutenant
Luther M; Overstreet. Lieutenant Orri'i
G. Murfln, Lieutenant George Cook Sweet.
Lieutenant Lloyd England, Captain C. C.
Clark, Dr. Garry, Mr. and Mrs. Maurice
Casey. Dr. and Mrs.. Ellinwood, Miss Flor
ence Ives,'. Miss Patricia . Cosgrave, Cap
tain Wolfe; Willialm Hoag and. Mrs. Cun
ningham. . _ .
Having spent the money accumulated
during two years' service in the army,
Paul Warren,' late a private In Troop C.
Eleventh Cavalry, went out to the Cliff
House yesterday afternoon and put a bul
let into his brain. Warren went into the
barroom of the resort and purchased a
drink of whisky. After drinking the liquor
he walked out on the balcony and stood
for a few moments looking seaward. He
returned to the barroom, bought and
drank another glass of liquor and re
marked-to A. CL Lester, the bartender,
that "it was a fine day."- He stood for a
few moments leaning over the bar and
then walked down to the lavatory in the
basement.
A few moments later a shot was heard.
The attendants rushed down the stairs
and found the lifeless body, of Warren in
a small room, with a bullet hole over his
right eye. Death had' evidently been in
stantaneous, i ' Â¦
The body was taken to the Morgue, and
a search of the clothing revealed an ex
cellent discharge from tne army. From it
it was learned that Warren wa^ from
Quincy, 111., 31 years of age and a stenog
rapher by occupation. He had been paid
$110 when mustered out. barkeeper Lester
of \ the Cliff stated that he had the ap
pearance of. a man who had been on a
protracted spree.

KILLS HIMSELF
AT CLIFF HOUSE
The Settlement of Many Questions of
Long Standing and of Annoy
ance to Public Made
Possible.
t -
The bill amending the revenue laws, in
troduced into the Legislature early in the
last session and finally passed in a much
altered form, has just been received at
the Assessor's office.
Assessor Dodge, in speaking of the rad
ical changes which the bill will effect in
the affairs of his office, states that the
meet important innovation concerns, the
collection, by seizure and sale, of taxes
on personal property not secured by real
estate. This duty was formerly exercised
bv the Assessor and now devolves upon
the Tax Collector. ' ,
Another amendment makes it the duty
of each person to file a sworn statement
with the Assessor as to whether 01 not
he is the owner of any taxable property.
It also gives the Assessor the authority to
subpoena and examine any person witn
reference to property which j may be
stored with such persons The effect of
tnis amendment is to settle matters of lit
igation resulting from a question of the
Assessor's right to exact sworn state
ments from warehouse men, with refer
ence to goods stored in their buildings.
Taxable property, from. outside the coun
ty, but from within tne State, will be sub
ject to taxation in this city if found here
the first Monday in March. The Super
visors are given greater authority also in
the matter of refunding taxes collected
through duplicate or erroneous assess
ments. In the past this matter has
caused much difficulty through the inex
plicitness of ihe law. â
Another change has been effected to
provide for the cancellation of the sale
of a piece of property, made for the non
payment of an unlawfully assessed poll
tax or for one paid, but not properly
noted. An amendment which will afford
popular relief provides that a delinquent
Tax Collector must give fifteen days' no
tice of the commencement of a suit for
payment of additional taxes levied after
the equalization of the State roll, when
the previously assessed tax i has been
paia. In m*ny cases this amounts to a
few cents only and the people of the city
have been caused considerable annoyance
and expense through their Ignorance . of
any such additional tax until they were
sueO by the Collector for a sum much
greater than the original delinquency,
through the addition of penalties and
CCfctS. - Â¦ . . Â¦
. "The amendments and new provisions,"
says Assessor Dodge, "are, in my opinion,
all in the right direction and tend to fa
cilitate and make more certain the op
eration of the revenue laws than was pos
sible heretofore."

BY NEW TAX BILL

Is Gained, by the
Amendments.
JOTJXMIT GOING OUT
TO PAINT INDIANS
A Bally at the Bohemian Club to
Give the Artist a
Send-Off.
Amedee Joullin, well known In the Cali
fornia guild of picture painters, is pre
paring for another expedition to the home
of the red man. Two years ag:o he visited
the tribes of New Mexico and Arizona and
painted pictures that won the admiration
of artists. On the journey in view he will
first visit the tribes in Idaho and Mon
tana and then travel to the south. He
v.-'ll paint the noble buck, the Industrious
squaw and the picturesque papoose and
bring home with hn* trophies and skins
for the studio. Imcfflnation? which is .an
excellent quality of the true artist, will
supply what he fans to see.
A dinner, or little send-off, to Mr. Joul
lin was given at the Bohemian Club last
evening. Assembled at the table round
were: Frank Gelett Burgess, Porter Gar
rett. Edpar Matthews, Robert I. Aitken,
Fremont Older. James H. Graham, F. E.
Beck. W. F. Metson. W. D. English, Ry
land B. Wallace. Judge Henry A. Melvln,
Dr. A. Warner, Dr. Russell H. Cool. E. A.
Brugipre, George H. Malter, Charles G.
Yale. George T. Bromley. Hugh M. Burke
and A. Joullin.
At the annual election of the Bohemian
Ciub yesterday Frank P. Deering was
elected president, John Landers
vice president, Charles K. Field secre
tary and John Dempster McKee treas
urer. The following named directors for
the year were chosen: Charles J. Dick
rr.an, D. Ernest Melliss, Arthur W. Moore
and Wallace A. Sabin.
Vanderlynn Stow, the retiring president
of the club, has served the institution
â¢with remarkable fidelity and cuceess for
a period embracing two regular terms.
During his administration of affairs the
plans for acquiring a grove of noble red
wood trees were put into practical opera
tion and carried to a successful finish. Â¦
The Bohemian Club -was the first insti
tution in the State to fully estimate the
grandeur and beauty of the redwood for
ests. The agitation for reserving a grove
began with the Inauguration of the mid
summer high jinks in the woods twenty
years ago. The eloquence of General "W.
H. L. Barnes, Frank M. Ptxley, John F.
Swift, John H. Boalt and Horace G. Platt
induced many members to subscribe to
the sentiment of preservation, but it was
reserved for .Mr. Stow's administration to
find purchasers for the bonds. It is high
ly creditable to the Bohemian Club as an
organization that it has saved to Califor
nia one of the most beautiful and majestic
groves of the famous Russian River for
est. The example of the club in this en
terprise will surely speak to other socie
ties of public spirited men.
They Axe Credited With Having
Saved the Renowned Grove of
Bsdvoods on the Eussian â¢
River.
Compliments for Vanderlynn
Stow and for His As
sociates. v

NEW YORK. April S.â The call loan rate
proved again to be the solar plexus of the
Flock market, as it has done on many oc
casions before. The constriction of the money
supply and the advance in the rate for call
money precipitated such a spasm of liquida
tion as threw the stock market into a con
dition bordering on demoralization. What
happened to-day in the stock market Is what.
according to all observers, was bound toÂ» hap
pen in due course of time. Nevertheless, as
is the wont of stock market happenings, the
day's event came as a surprise. Even the
most enthusiastic bull has agreed to the prop
osition that such a rampant speculation ae
has been going on for weeks past was bound
to overdo itself and be followed by a relapse
of corresponding violence. Only the enthusi
astic bull has clung to the supposition that
the relapse was still remote and has continu
ally moved forward the supposed date of its
occurrence in the hope that there was time
for Mill another Fpet'ulative turn. Even the
wild and excited market of last week, which
â¢was an ominous symptom, left the bulls un
prepared for to-day's developments. Tips for
a rise i.i many stocks were still widely preva
lent before the opening this morning and the
explanation was offered that Thursday's flurry
in call loan rates was due to manipulation
by the bears. But the instant the market
opened It became evident that the develop
ments tince Thursday bad induced a wide
spread determination to sell stocks. Another
palpable fact was that some of the most pow
erful operators In the market had already sold
their holdings and taken profits during the
wild excitement of last week and were com
pactly organized on the bear side of the mar
ket this morning. Perceiving the "highly vul
nerable condition of the market owing to the
over-extension of long accounts thinly mar
ginsd and backed by weak resources, the
bears fell upon the market with great fierce
ness and cut a wide breach in prices at thÂ«
lirs-t onslaught. The- wide extent of the first
declines, v.hich were made by successive drops
from 1 to VA points between sales. Invited
quick profit-taking by the bears and the vio
iont rallies which ensued encouraged the bulls
to make 'some stand against the reduction,
Jioiung that the flurry was temporary and the
market prepared to renew the advance. This
was the only period of the day when there
was any show of positive strength, and Sugar
People s Gae, Texas and Pacific and a number
of less important stocks were marked ud
sharply over last Thursday's level.- The
strength was short lived, however, and after
the demand from the bears had been satisfied
tnany of the banks began calling loans. This

CHICAGO. April 8. â For a moment at the
opening the Corn market resembled to a degree
the wildness which characterized the market
last week._ Seasonable weather and liberal re
ceipts caused a rush to sell at the start, and
May ranged from 44 to 43%c, }s@%c under Sat
urday's closing price. Greater weakness had
been expected, and when it failed to materialize
shorts covered and the market worked back to
44%@44%c. Profit-taking by country holders
caused a reaction to 43%@43%c The Wheat
strength caused a rally from this to 44%c, and
the close was firm, with May Va@%c higher at
44%@44%c. The volume of business was very
much less than recently. Phillips did but little.
Â¦ "Wheat was still feeling thn bearishness of
Snow's crop report at: the opening to-day. The
sentiment was further weakened by seasonable
weather, liberal receipts and an ! increase on
passage. Bears were active at the start, May
selling from 70% to. 70>4c. %@%c under the
previous close. A report that German Wheat
had suffered from frost and a similar rumor
from California started shorts covering. It de
veloped that the market had been sold to a
standstill. Very little Wheat was to be had,
and May, reacted sharply to 70%c. A visible
decrease of 859,000 bushels, lighter primary re
ceipts, heavy clearances and good export de
mand later Imparted additional strength, and
May rallied to 71V4c. Commission houses bold
on the advance, but the close was firm, with
May %c higher at 71',ic. It was reported later
that the California frosts had done little dam
age to grains. The Orange Judd Farmer re
ported that the 'condition ot winter Wheat Is
practically perfect in the centers of heavy pro
duction, the general average being 95.5. against
85.7 at the same date for me past seven years.
The market for Oats was dull. May closed a
shade down at 2o%@25 5 / sC.
Provisions were dull and weak, May Pork
leadipg the decline under liquidation by out
side holders to secure what profits remained.
May Pork closed 35c under Saturday, Lard 10c
down and Ribs 2Vic depressed.

' Dairy Exchange, Quotations.
Xt the fially meeting of the Ban Francisco
Dairy Exchange yesterday lt was unanimously
voted that In view of certain newspaper
charses that the exchange was manipulating
the market Quotations for Butter, Cheese and
Eggs, State Dairy Commissioner Vanderbilt
be Invited to examine the methods of the ex
change, and that If he found the charges true
the exchange would cease to make quotations.
In the contrary event they would be estab
lished every day as at. present.

"Coffee has developed a weak position since
March 7 and prices for Brazils in New York
are where they were prior to the speculative
movement there, showing a net loss of %c
per pound during, the month undar review.
Brazil receipts are too heavy, coffee stocks
are too large to be Ignored, and all consum
ing markets are showing the Â¦ effect of over
supplies. Local: business has utterly turned
quiet as buyers deem conservatism the best
for their Interests, especially in view of the
recent large arrivals here, with the prospect of
further liberal additions during the next sixty
days. â¢ ' â¢

MARRIAGE OF FOPTJXAIt,
YOUNG BUSINESS MAW
$5000 promissory' note given to Miss Lulie
H. Holcomb, now Mrs. O'Shea, and his
sister, Lydia S. Holcomb, by Peter L.
and Harriet Saunders, formed the basis
of sensational charges against the payees
in Judge Hall's court to-day.
Miss Holcomb and Mrs. O'Shea brought
suit to foreclose on the note and secured
judgment on March 9. To-day the Saun
derses presented a motion for a new trial,
alleging that the note was forged. Judge
Hall decided that there was no evidence
to support the accusation, and denied the
motion. His Honor reprimanded the de
fendants for their method of attacking
persons of standing in the community,
and said that it was no part of his In
tention to allow his court to be made
use of to evade the payment of just ob
ligations.

Police officers went in search of the ele
vator boy, but could not locate him. Xo
one about the building could give any in
formation regarding him except that he
had been employed in the building only a
day or two.
A hurried examination showed that Mr.
West was still alive, and he was hurried

lance, but life was extinct before he
reached there.
The old eentleman was thrown off his
balance, and he was caught between the
floor of the cage and the wall of the ele
vator 6haft and dragged upward several
feet. By this time the elevator boy had
brought the cage to a standstill, and- the
body of the unfortunate man was dropped
to the stone floor below.
Orson C. West, an aged rancher residing
near Napa City, met an awful death by
being crushed by an elevator in the Phe
lan building yesterday afternoon. Mr.
West and his ron, Alfred L. W r est. an in
surance agent, entered the building at
about 6 o'clock to go to the younger man's
offices. The son stepped Into the elevator
and his father, who was close behind
him, had just got one foot Jnside the door
when the elevator suddenly shot upward.

FATALLY MANGLED
BY AN ELEVATOR
The buffet car that has been found to
be the source of much complaint will still
be on the run, but improvements in Its
management have already been ordered.
In the future passengers occupying seats
in the ' day coaches or parlor car will be
served at their seats by the porters, - if
they so desire. Tables -will be placed
between the seats and patrons will not
have to go to the cafe car for lunch or
dinner should the cafe car be overcrowded.
A regular time table will be adopted for
the new local train. between here and Los
Angeles on the coast d- vision to-day.- .
General Manager Kruttschnitt held- a
conference yesterday w'th a number of
railroad officials and rjlans were laid for
the putting on of another local train to re
lieve the heavy traflic on the coast lino
This train is the oniy remedy that can
be made at present. It is impossible for
the limited to carry more cars and make
the time that it runs on now. The latter
train is made ui> of one parlor car, two
first-class" day coaches, a smoking and
buffet car, a postal and baggage car. The
New York Central Limited Express, which
is considered the fastest train in the
world, only, hauls five cars, and yet the
limited that travels between here and Log
Angeles makes nearly as good time. By
putting more cars on this train would
only mean that 'a longer; time schedule
would have to be made. "This the South
ern Pacific Company is reluctant to do,
hence the additional local train.
Since the opening of this line the traffic
has been enormous, especially from Los
Angeles to this city. All the tourist travel,
which is very large at this time of the
year, has come over this road. The con
sequence is that the trains each day have
been overcrowded, and the buffet car that
takes the place of a "diner 1 V has been
found inadequate to serve the. patrons.
The Call last Sunday called attention ex
clusively to the many inconveniences that
strangers are put io in the run up from
Los Angeles, and evidently the article had
the desired effect.
The Southern Pacific Company will at
once put on another local train on the
new coast division between this city and
Los Angeles to accommodate the in
creased traffic.
Owing to Heavy Traffic on
the New Road a Local
Is to Be Added. â¢

FOR COAST LINE

HARRISON, Neb., April 8.â Miss Lizzie
E. Cottman, teacher in the district school
near here, alone, facing a torrent, of
water carrying trees, dead animals and
all the debris borne on a flood; to-day
rescued the pupils of her school and pre
served Â« the building itself from what
seemed inevitable destruction.
Without warning the TVhite River over
flowed its banks, surrounded the school
building and threatened to carry it away.
Miss Cottman waded in water waist deep
to where a horse was -picketed some dis
trnce away! Returningvwith the animal,
she harnessed the horse to the building,
which was shaken from its foundations,
?.nd thR horse's strength held the school
house in place. Help came from the
neighbors in three-quarters of an hour. ;

taken. ,

COURAGEOUS TEACHER
SAVES LIVES OF PUPILS
DENVER. April 8. â Nellie Rivlerre,
wife of Emil Rivierre of 720 South Tenth
street, who was said to have run away
with their twelve-year-old boy last Tues
day night,' has been mourned as dead by
her relatives in New York, and a grave
in Greenwood Cemetery has been dug to
receive her body. It was a trick played
by her husband, she says, to obtain money
to pay her expenses after desertion.
Last Tuesday night, according to her
statement, she left home for a few. min
utes and returned to find that her husband
had gone and taken Rena, their little boy,
with him. She found a note from her
husband saying he had gone, to New York
to attend a trial and would return. He
gave her directions to write, but instead
she telegraphed to her aunt, Mrs. Patter
son of 33 Monroe place. New York.' tell
ing that Rivierre had deserted her.
"I received the answer .this morning,"
she said. "Mrs. Patterson's letter stated
that Rivierre had written them that I was
ill and that the worst might be- expected.
The letter also said that a few days later
a- telegram was received from him sayinp
I was dead and to have a grave dug in
Greenwood Cemetery. With it was a re
quest for money to ship the body." '
Mrs. Patterson said that she had sent
$150: that the grave had been dug. and
that the family had ordered mourning
dresses. Then came Mrs. Rivierre's tele
gram, unconsciously exposing the trick.
The .Rivierres came to Denver from New
York about Christmas for Mrs. Rivierre's
health. The husband is an insurance
agent and an- expert bicycle rider. Mrs.
Rivierre says her aunt in New York is
prominent and Is worth about $700,000. She
will remain in Denver in the hope of find
ing some trace of her child.
Woman in Denver Tells a Sensational
Story About Actions â¢ of Her
Missing Spouse.
SAYS HUSBAND CAUSED
A GRAVE TO BE DUG
Thomas Lanigan. barkeeper for Camp
bell & Benson, Sansome and Pacific
streets, was arrested yesterday afternoon
by Policeman Skain and booked at the
City Prison on a charge of robbery. The
complaining witness is Michael Ryan, a
Fwitohman from Oakland.
Ryan said he was walking along Pacific
street shortly after 8 o'clock yesteray
morning: when he met Lanigan. who was
standing- outside the saloon. Ryan offered
to throw dice with Lanigan for 50 cents
a throw, but Lanigan doubted if he had
any money. Ryan lapped his vest pocket
and remarked he had plenty of money.
Then Lanigan made a grab for the vest
pocket, tearing it and getting possession
of $40. with which he skipped.
Ryan waited around the saloon till Lan
ijran made his appearance about 2 o'clock
in the afternoon, and then notified Police
man Skain. who placed Lanigan under
arrest. Lanigan denies the charge and
says Ryan is romancing.

Episcopal Church.
ALAMEDA. April 8.â The wedding of
Miss Viola Pattiani and Dr. Lewis Mace
of San Jose took place this evening and
was attended by a large number of the
society friends of the young couple.
Christ Episcopal Church, the scene of the
wedding, was beautifully decorated with
lilies and smilax/ Rev. Dr. T. J. Lacey,
the rector, performed the ceremony. The
gown of the bride was a beautiful crea
tion of white satin with pearl passemen
terie, en train. She carried white lilies
of the valley. Miss Estelle Pattiani was
the maid of honor and she wore a dress
of silk mull over white taffeta. Miss
Emma Moffltt, Miss Susan Edson, Miss
Irma Lyndon and Miss Ida Pattiani were
the bridesmaids. The Misses Elois and
Genevieve Pattiani. the little sisters of
the bride, were flower bearers. The best
man was Fred Barstow and" Dr. Grissen.
Gilmore Russell, Fred Schneider and
Charles Schneider were the ushers.
After the ceremony there was a re
ception at the Pattiani home, corner Cen
tral avenue and Cottage street. The
house decorations were white roses,
palms and smllax. The guests sat down
to a banquet table set in red and green.
The bride is the eldest daughter of Mr.
and Mrs. A. W. Pattiani, and Is an ac
complished amateur actress. She was a
member of the class of 1900 at the Uni
versity of California and as the leading
lady in the Junior farce, "James. Wob
berts. Freshman," scored a hit. Dr. Mace
ia a graduate of Stanford University and
of Bellevue College of New York.

Fitzhugh Lee is a courteous gentleman
of modest bearing. His unobtrusive man
ner about the hotel lobby, where he was
aENERAL FITZHUGH LEE. ac
companied by his daughter, Miss
Anne Lee, arrived here yesterday
from Denver and is staying at the
Occidental Hotel. The telegraphic
reports that have recently been published
about the illness of the general are only
too true. Lee is, a very sick man and is
fighting off the results of a bad attack of
la grippe which he contracted recently.
He still shows signs of his severe illness.
He says he will remain here some time, as
he thinks that the climate of California
is ideal, and the State, from the little he
has seen of it, the garden spot of the
earth. . .{

Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1118 Broadway, April 8.
One of the Important post-Lenten social
affairs of this city will be the reception
to be given April 17 by the Ebell Society
in honor of Mrs. Benjamin Ide Wheeler
of Berkeley. The event will take place at
the Ebell Society's rooms, and opportu
nity will be afforded for many of Oak
land's prominent leaders in social, edu
cational and philanthropic life to meet the
wife of the president of the University of
California. Mrs. Wheeler has been active
along these lines since her arrival in Cali
fornia, " and it is in recognition of her
eminent standing that the Ebell Society

a center of attraction, won him the ad

"I have had a bad attack of la grippe,"
said the general last evening, "but I think
if there is anything that will bring me
back to my normal self it is. your beautiful
climate. I have not decided how long I
will remain out here. I am simply touring
to benefit my health."
General Fitzhugh Lee is one of the best
known and most admired of living- Ameri
cans. He was born at Clermont, Fairfax
County, Va.. in 1835, and is a nephew of
the late General Robert E. Lee. He grad
uated from West Point in 1856 and re
mained in the United States army until
the breaking out of the civil war. He
then resigned and. joining the Confederate
army, served as adjutant general to Gen
eral Ewell. He later commanded a cav
alry corps in Northern Virginia. He sur
rendered to General Meade at the close of
the war and was one of the first to accept
the armistice. He was elected Governor
of Virginia in 1886, serving till 1890. He
was appointed United States' Consul to
Havana from 1S93 until the breaking out
of the Spanish war, when he returned to
the United States and was placed in com
mand of the Seventh Army Corps. After
peace was declared. It was mutually
agreed by both powers that he should be
come military governor of Havana.

The Board . of Supervisors yesterday
passed to print an ordinance removing the
present limitations upon the height of fire
proof and non-fireproof buildings. The
amended ordinance limits the height of
buildings of the first "named class to 170
feet and of the second to 104 feet.
Architect Mooser explained that a num
ber of tine buildings were being projected,
but the present -ordinance militated
against; their erection on account of the
height restriction."-
The ordinance, making it a misdemeanor
for any person attempting to secure the
benefits of the pupils' half-fare ordinance
who is not entitled thereto was passed to
print. The Geary Street Railway Com
pany notified the board that it would
abide by the half-fare ordinance, but it
denies the constitutionality thereof.
The ordinance regulating the use of
signs and transparencies which was sub
muted by the Merchants' Association was
passed to print. An amendment prohibit
ing the maintenance of -illuminated signs
for advertising purposes was lost. Bran
deristein gave notice of reconsideration.
A communication was received from S.
\V. Hollaciay, oirenng to sell the lot on the
northeast corner, or' Clay and Octavia
streets, running half way to Sacramento
street, for 540,OuO, and the next lot on the
east for $35,000. The land is desired by
tho Park Commission to add to Lafayette
Park, which plan is recommended in a
letter submitted to the board.
The ofter of William H. Greene and
George \V. Greene to deed a right of way
for a culvert under Nineteenth avenue
was referred to -the Judiciary Committee.
The Board of Public Works recommends
the acceptance of the grant.
The ordinance establishing grades on
Army and Dolores streets was passed to
print.
The ordinance prohibiting the. papering,
tinting and kalsomining of walls in build
ings without iirst removing the old paper
tiritinsr and kalsomine therefrom, was re
ferred to the Judiciary Committee.
The City Attorney was directed to ren
der an opinion whether the Tax Collector,
has authority to engage special counsel to
prosecute suits for the collection of de
linquent license taxes on nickel machines
owned by the Pacific Telephone Company.
Colonel A. D. Cutler addressed the
board with a request for. the return of a
penalty imposed by the Assessor for fail
ing to make n sworn statement. Cutler
explained that he had never been noti
fied of the arbitrary assessment, but his
petition was denied.

The protest of Henry Warfteld, secre
tary of the Anti-Bondage League, against
t he issuance of bonds to provide for pub
lic improvements was referred to the
Kinanet Committee. His reasons for the
protest are. among others, that bond is
sues as proposed will more than double
the cost of the improvements.
The resolution directing the City Attor
ney to discontinue further action in* the
condemnation proceedings in connection
with St. Mary's square until the final de
cision is handed down by the Supreme
Court was adopted.
The Mayor was authorized to appoint a
committee of twenty-fire citizens to take
charge of the Memorial day exercises.
McCarthy suggested that the result of
the investigation into the Police Court
scandals be heard before any action be
taken. Reed f=a id that the city was pro
tected by bonds exacted from city of
ficials, and th > District Attorney should
see that his subordinates obey the law by
turning the foes collected into the city
tieasury every day.
Supervisor Comte presented a resolu
tion, which was adopted, which directs
the City Attorney to advise the board as
to what proceedings are necessary to call
for a bond flection for hospitals, schools
and sewers and for park extension by ac
quisition of land?; also as to whether the
board has power to acquire land for park
purposes by purchase or condemnation;
also whether all propositions for the issu
ance of bends be submitted at the same
election and on the same ballot.
McCarthy desired to know what special
necessity existed for the creation of the
position. He referred to the fact that the
board already had an expert! Jennings
stated that the Finance Committee rec
ommended the appointment because the
Grand Jury as a body had requested it.
BrandenstPin desired to know out of what
fund the salary should be paid, and when
informed that it would come out of the
urgent necessity fund opposed the bill on
the ground thut it made another inroad
oÂ» the fund named.

offices as to tho fees 'collected was vigor
ously opposed at yesterday's meeting of
the Board of Supervisors and was defeat

SAN BERNARDINO, April* 8.â Edward
Seeley, 21 years of age, son of Randolph
Seeley. a wealthy, rancher of this county

Pacific Train.
SANTA ROSA; April 8.â Among the ar
rivals on this morning's Southern Pacific
train from the East was a well dressed
young, woman, who had become suddenly
demented on a train at Ogclen. As she
had a ticket for Santa Rosa and her place
of residence was not known, she was put
in charge of one of the company's of
ficials and brought here. .She would not
talk and her case seemed a difficult one to
handle, Her ticket was made out: In the
name of P. McSweaney, and the young
woman is supposed to fce from some town
In Iowar:She says she hae relatives here,
but* will not tell who' they are. .Sheriff;
Grace is now endeavoring to learn some
thing about her-relatives by wire. In the
meantime Bhe Is being properly cared for.

Scenes From "Julius Caesar."
To-morrow and Thursday evenings the'
Lincoln Club of the San Francisco Settle
ment Association will present, under the
management of J. I. Housman, scenes
from the play "Julius Caesar" in the as
sociation's new quarters at 86 South Park.
Tickets can be had at Elder & Shepard's
book store,. 23S Post street; the Bella Vista
Hotel, and at the box office on the even
ings of the performance.

start newspapers.

Board of Public Works.'' You will be
surprised at the result.

A host of witnesses, embracing former
Police Judges, attaches of Police Courts
and men conversant with the details of
Police Court practices, has been summon
ed to anoear before the Grand Jury to
night. Each one of these is supposed to
have Intimate knowledge of the way

mony.
Money Still Held in Treasury.
The tangle regarding the payment out
of the treasury of the moneys deposited
as ball still continues. Treasurer Brooks
will pay .no court orders. Auditor Wells
will not audit them, and not until City
and County Attorney Lane gives an opin
ion will those who have money in the
â¢rlty treasury obtain what belongs to them.
Police Judges refuse to sjgn orders for the
release of bail money until the o.uestion
Is settled. One exception in, however,
made to this rule,for the convenience of
the Police Court clerks: When the
amount of the line equals the amount of
the bail deposited, the order is signed and
sent as a voucher to the County Clerk.
These arc ax-cumulating rapidly, and the
County Clerk looks for all kinds of trou
ble at the end of the month if some plan
is not devised to straighten the matter
out. All the vouchers ndw in Clerk
Deane's custody represent money actual
ly in deposit in the city treasury, and it
is only a small matter of bookkeeping to
get the accounts straightened out. But
Treasurer Brooks is obstinate. Orders for
the personal withdrawals of money are
held up in the same manner; so County
Clerk Deane has a mass of sympathizers.
Judge Cabaniss has been endeavoring to
secure the appointment by Mayor Phelan
of a Justice of the Peace to take his place

amination of Sullivan comes up to-mor
row morning. He has had several confer
ences with Secretarv Fay, apparently be
ing unable to meet the luayor in his of-
Jice. From conversations had with Fay,
Judge Cabajiiss thinks that the Mayor
will act and appoint Justice of tne Peace
Percy V. Long to act instead of himself.

the warrant and bond clerk in a measure
from the financial complications of his

part of the report of the Grand Jury. He
will be an additional attache of the office,
under heavy bonds, ai>d will receive all
moneys. Â¦

, SANTA MONICANS FIXE
TWO MORE PROTESTS
Do Not Want Railroad Track to
Englewood Abandoned. by
. . Santa Fe.
Two protests were filed yesterday with
the. Railroad Commissioners by Seward
Cole, secretary of the Cahuenga Valley
Tvtmon Colony, and W. S. Vawter of Santa
Monica against the abandonment of the
railroad track that at present is being op
erated by the Santa Fe Railway between
Santa Monica and Englewood.
Both protests claim that should the
Railroad Commissioners sanction the pe
tition of the Santa Fe to stop operating
the road and abandon the track, fruit
prowers would be put to much inconveni
ence and financial loss. The matter has
been under the consideration of the Rail
road Commissioners for several weeks and
will be acted upon b,y them at their next
regular meeting. â¢ -â¢

Homer Lee, a former Stanford student,
who went to China a little more than a
year ago, and who is now a general in the
Imperial Array of the Emperor of China,
is at present a guest at the Palace.
Lee has recently returned from China
and is on his way to Washington and
London to attempt to Induce the "Amer
ican and English governments to inter
vene and again place the Emperor of
China on the throne. He says the Dow
ager Empress is the cause of all the
trouble in China and that she is receiv
ing the moral support of the Russian
Government. Lee carries with him a
number of ghastly photographs, illus
trating the barbaric manner in which the
Chinese are executed at present by the
Empress, which not only includes the be
heading of victims, but also the mutila
tion of the bodies by cutting off the arms
and legs. Lee claims that one of the in
novations of the Emperor before he was
dethroned and â¢ imprisoned was the pro
hibiting of any more of those acts of bar
barity. ,.
"You must understand that China is
divided into two sections," he said. "The
Yangtse River forms the division. All
that lies north of this river is governed
by the Dowager Empress and all south of
it by the Emperor, or at least, by his fol
lowing. The 'Boxer' movement was sim
ply an uprising instigated by the Queen,
who was coaxed into it by the Russians,
who' think 'by war and strife they will
eventually secure control of China.

BOYCOTTS
V" I wfsh to announce to. the public that i am boy-
cotted for not closing my store at 6 p. m., and wish to
say that I am in sympathy with the.Gerks' efforts for
shorter hours, but think they can accomplish that with-
out closing my Store.
My clerk is off at 6 p. m., but I cannot make* my
expenses by closing at that hour.
In justice to myself and customers I am com-
pelled to remain open until 8 p. m., but do not ask any
one to work for me. I conduct a small Men's Furnish-
ing Goods Store at 1204 Market street, where the new-
est and best apparel for men can be found at reason-
able prices, and hereby appeal to the liberal-minded
public for their moral support and patronage in this
struggle. I sell good honest goods for ! good honest
prices, and try to be honest and reasonable myself.
Hoping you' will be impressed with this statement
of facts, and that I may have a 'share of your patron-
age, I am sincerely yours,
JOHN T. GIBSON,
1204 Market Street.
